During the 1930s at Bell Laboratories, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/William-Shockley" class="md-crosslink">William Shockley</a>, later coinventor of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/transistor" class="md-crosslink">transistor</a>, had demonstrated a device—a tube, called a <span id="ref723672"></span>delay line, containing water and ethylene glycol—for effecting a predictable delay in information <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/transmission-engineering" class="md-crosslink autoxref">transmission</a>. Eckert had already built and experimented in 1943 with such a delay line (using mercury) in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/conjunction-logic" class="md-crosslink autoxref">conjunction</a> with <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/radar" class="md-crosslink autoxref">radar</a> research, and sometime in 1944 he hit upon the new idea of placing a quartz crystal at each end of the mercury delay line in order to sustain and modify the resulting pattern. In effect, he invented a new storage device. Whereas ENIAC required one tube per bit, EDVAC could use a delay line and 10 vacuum tubes to store 1,000 bits. Before the invention of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/magnetic-core-storage" class="md-crosslink autoxref">magnetic core memory</a> and the transistor, which would eliminate the need for vacuum tubes altogether, the mercury delay line was instrumental in increasing computer storage and reliability.</p><!--[P79]--></section><span id="ref216032" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216047" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">Von Neumann’s “Preliminary Discussion”</h2><p>But the design of the modern, or classical, computer did not fully crystallize until the publication of a 1946 paper by Arthur Burks, <span id="ref724921"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Herman-Goldstine" class="md-crosslink">Herman Goldstine</a>, and <span id="ref723673"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/John-von-Neumann" class="md-crosslink">John von Neumann</a> titled “<span id="ref723674"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Preliminary-Discussion-of-the-Logical-Design-of-an-Electronic-Computing-Instrument" class="md-crosslink">Preliminary Discussion of the Logical Design of an Electronic Computing Instrument</a>”. Although the paper was essentially a synthesis of ideas currently “in the air,” it is frequently cited as the birth certificate of computer science.</p><!--[P80]--><p>Among the principles enunciated in the paper were that data and instructions should be kept in a single <span id="ref723675"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/stored-program-concept" class="md-crosslink">store</a> and that instructions should be encoded so as to be modifiable by other instructions. This was an extremely critical decision, because it meant that one program could be treated as data by another program. Zuse had considered and rejected this possibility as too dangerous. But its inclusion by von Neumann’s group made possible high-level programming languages and most of the advances in software of the following 50 years. Subsequently, computers with stored programs would be known as von Neumann machines.</p><!--[P81]--><p>One problem that the stored-program idea solved was the need for rapid access to instructions. Colossus and ENIAC had used plugboards, which had the advantage of enabling the instructions to be read in electronically, rather than by much slower mechanical card readers, but it also had the disadvantage of making these first-generation machines very hard to program. But if the instructions could be stored in the same electronic memory that held the data, they could be accessed as quickly as needed. One immediately obvious consequence was that EDVAC would need a lot more memory than ENIAC.</p><!--[P82]--></section><span id="ref216032" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216048" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The first stored-program machines</h2><p>Government secrecy hampered British efforts to build on wartime computer advances, but engineers in Britain still beat the Americans to the goal of building the first stored-program <span id="ref724777"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/digital-computer" class="md-crosslink">digital computer.</a> At the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/University-of-Manchester" class="md-crosslink autoxref">University of Manchester</a>, <span id="ref723677"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Frederic-Calland-Williams" class="md-crosslink">Frederic C. Williams</a> and <span id="ref723680"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Tom-Kilburn" class="md-crosslink">Tom Kilburn</a> built a simple stored-program computer, known as the Baby, in 1948. This was built to test their invention of a way to store information on a <span id="ref723678"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/cathode-ray-tube" class="md-crosslink">cathode-ray tube</a> that enabled direct access (in contrast to the mercury delay line’s sequential access) to stored information. Although faster than Eckert’s storage method, it proved somewhat unreliable. Nevertheless, it became the preferred storage method for most of the early computers worldwide that were not already committed to mercury delay lines.</p><!--[P83]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="19237"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/24/23624-004-1455AE15.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/24/23624-004-1455AE15.jpg" alt="The Manchester Mark I, the first stored-program digital computer, c. 1949." data-width="453" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The Manchester Mark I, the first stored-program digital computer, c. 1949.</span><cite>Reprinted with permission of the Department of Computer Science, University of Manchester, Eng.</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML83]--><p>By 1949 Williams and Kilburn had extended the Baby to a full-size computer, the <span id="ref723676"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Manchester-Mark-I" class="md-crosslink">Manchester Mark I</a>. This had two major new features that were to become computer standards: a two-level store and instruction modification registers (which soon evolved into index registers). A magnetic drum was added to provide a random-access secondary storage device. Until machines were fitted with index registers, every instruction that referred to an address that varied as the program ran—e.g., an array element—had to be preceded by instructions to alter its address to the current required value. Four months after the Baby first worked, the British government contracted the electronics firm of Ferranti to build a production computer based on the prospective Mark I. This became the <span id="ref723679"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Ferranti-Mark-I" class="md-crosslink">Ferranti Mark I</a>—the first commercial computer—of which nine were sold.</p><!--[P84]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="19238"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/25/23625-004-DE225F47.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/25/23625-004-DE225F47.jpg" alt="Tom Kilburn standing beside the console of the Ferranti Mark I computer, c. 1950." data-width="436" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">Tom Kilburn standing beside the console of the Ferranti Mark I computer, <em>c.</em> 1950.</span><cite>Reprinted with permission of the Department of Computer Science, University of Manchester, Eng.</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML84]--><p>Kilburn, Williams, and colleagues at Manchester also came up with a breakthrough that would revolutionize how a computer executed instructions: they made it possible for the address portion of an instruction to be modified while the program was running. Before this, an instruction specified that a particular action—say, addition—was to be performed on data in one or more particular locations. Their innovation allowed the location to be modified as part of the operation of executing the instruction. This made it very easy to address elements within an array sequentially.</p><!--[P85]--><p>At the University of Cambridge, meanwhile, <span id="ref723681"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Maurice-Vincent-Wilkes" class="md-crosslink">Maurice Wilkes</a> and others built what is recognized as the first full-size, fully electronic, stored-program computer to provide a formal computing service for users. The <span id="ref723682"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/EDSAC" class="md-crosslink">Electronic Delay Storage Automatic Calculator</a> (EDSAC) was built on the set of principles synthesized by von Neumann and, like the Manchester Mark I, became operational in 1949. Wilkes built the machine chiefly to study programming issues, which he realized would become as important as the hardware details.</p><!--[P86]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="19231"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/18/23618-004-6F2B4A80.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/18/23618-004-6F2B4A80.jpg" alt="The EDSAC computer, 1947, with designer Maurice Wilkes (kneeling in the centre of the photograph)." data-width="385" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The EDSAC computer, 1947, with designer Maurice Wilkes (kneeling in the centre of the photograph).</span><cite>Courtesy of the Computer History Museum, Mountain View, CA</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML86]--></section><span id="ref216032" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216049" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref723683"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Whirlwind-computer" class="md-crosslink">Whirlwind</a></h2><p>New hardware continued to be invented, though. In the United States, <span id="ref724735"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Jay-Wright-Forrester" class="md-crosslink">Jay Forrester</a> of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT) and Jan Aleksander Rajchman of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/RCA-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Radio Corporation of America</a> came up with a new kind of memory based on magnetic cores that was fast enough to enable MIT to build the first <span id="ref723684"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/real-time-operation" class="md-crosslink">real-time</a> computer, Whirlwind. A real-time computer is one that can respond seemingly instantly to basic instructions, thus allowing an operator to interact with a “running” computer.</p><!--[P87]--></section></section><span id="ref216032" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216050" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref723685"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/UNIVAC" class="md-crosslink">UNIVAC</a></h2><p>After leaving the Moore School, <span id="ref723687"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/J-Presper-Eckert-Jr" class="md-crosslink">Eckert</a> and <span id="ref723689"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/John-Mauchly" class="md-crosslink">Mauchly</a> struggled to obtain capital to build their latest design, a computer they called the Universal Automatic Computer, or UNIVAC. (In the meantime, they contracted with the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Northrop-Grumman-Corporation" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Northrop Corporation</a> to build the <span id="ref723690"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Binary-Automatic-Computer" class="md-crosslink">Binary Automatic Computer</a>, or BINAC, which, when completed in 1949, became the first American stored-program computer.) The partners delivered the first UNIVAC to the U.S. Bureau of the Census in March 1951, although their company, their patents, and their talents had been acquired by Remington Rand, Inc., in 1950. Although it owed something to experience with ENIAC, UNIVAC was built from the start as a stored-program computer, so it was really different architecturally. It used an operator keyboard and console typewriter for input and magnetic tape for all other input and output. Printed output was recorded on tape and then printed by a separate tape printer.</p><!--[P88]--><p>The UNIVAC I was designed as a commercial data-processing computer, intended to replace the punched-card accounting machines of the day. It could read 7,200 decimal digits per second (it did not use binary numbers), making it by far the fastest business machine yet built. Its use of Eckert’s mercury delay lines greatly reduced the number of vacuum tubes needed (to 5,000), thus enabling the main processor to occupy a “mere” 14.5 by 7.5 by 9 feet (approximately 4.4 by 2.3 by 2.7 metres) of space. It was a true business machine, signaling the convergence of academic computational research with the office <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/automation" class="md-crosslink autoxref">automation</a> trend of the late 19th and early 20th centuries. As such, it ushered in the era of “Big Iron”—or large, mass-produced computing equipment.</p><!--[P89]--></section></section><section id="ref216051" data-level="2"><h2 class="h2">The age of <span id="ref723691"></span>Big Iron</h2><p>A snapshot of computer development in the early 1950s would have to show a number of companies and laboratories in competition—technological competition and increasingly earnest business competition—to produce the few computers then demanded for scientific research. Several computer-building projects had been launched immediately after the end of World War II in 1945, primarily in the United States and Britain. These projects were inspired chiefly by a 1946 document, “<span id="ref723692"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Preliminary-Discussion-of-the-Logical-Design-of-an-Electronic-Computing-Instrument" class="md-crosslink">Preliminary Discussion of the Logical Design of an Electronic Digital Computing Instrument</a>,” produced by a group working under the direction of mathematician <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/John-von-Neumann" class="md-crosslink">John von Neumann</a> of the <span id="ref723693"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Institute-for-Advanced-Study" class="md-crosslink">Institute for Advanced Study</a> at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Princeton-University" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Princeton University</a>. The IAS paper, as von Neumann’s document became known, articulated the concept of the stored program—a concept that has been called the single largest innovation in the history of the computer. (Von Neumann’s principles are described earlier, in the section <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref216045" class="md-crosslink">Toward the classical computer</a>.) Most computers built in the years following the paper’s distribution were designed according to its plan, yet by 1950 there were still only a handful of working stored-program computers.</p><!--[P90]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="19213"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/01/23601-004-CD74EAC4.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/01/23601-004-CD74EAC4.jpg" alt="An IBM 650 computer system, c. 1954Relatively inexpensive, compact, and easy to operate, the IBM 650 quickly became the most widely used computer for business applications." data-width="379" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">An IBM 650 computer system, c. 1954Relatively inexpensive, compact, and easy to operate, the IBM 650 quickly became the most widely used computer for business applications.</span><cite>IBM Archives</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML90]--><p>Business use at this time was marginal because the machines were so hard to use. Although computer makers such as Remington Rand, the Burroughs Adding Machine Company, and <span id="ref723695"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Business-Machines-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">IBM</a> had begun building machines to the IAS specifications, it was not until 1954 that a real market for business computers began to emerge. The <span id="ref723694"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/IBM-650" class="md-crosslink">IBM 650</a>, delivered at the end of 1954 for colleges and businesses, was a decimal implementation of the IAS design. With this low-cost magnetic drum computer, which sold for about $200,000 apiece (compared with about $1,000,000 for the scientific model, the IBM 701), IBM had a hit, eventually selling about 1,800 of them. In addition, by offering universities that taught computer science courses around the IBM 650 an academic discount program (with price reductions of up to 60 percent), IBM established a cadre of engineers and programmers for their machines. (Apple later used a similar discount strategy in American grade schools to capture a large proportion of the early microcomputer market.)</p><!--[P91]--><p>A snapshot of the era would also have to show what could be called the sociology of computing. The actual use of computers was restricted to a small group of trained experts, and there was resistance to the idea that this group should be expanded by making the machines easier to use. Machine time was expensive, more expensive than the time of the mathematicians and scientists who needed to use the machines, and computers could process only one problem at a time. As a result, the machines were in a sense held in higher regard than the scientists. If a task could be done by a person, it was thought that the machine’s time should not be wasted with it. The public’s perception of computers was not positive either. If motion pictures of the time can be used as a guide, the popular image was of a room-filling brain attended by white-coated technicians, mysterious and somewhat frightening—about to eliminate jobs through automation.</p><!--[P92]--><p>Yet the machines of the early 1950s were not much more capable than Charles Babbage’s <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref216027" class="md-crosslink">Analytical Engine</a> of the 1830s (although they were much faster). Although in principle these were general-purpose computers, they were still largely restricted to doing tough math problems. They often lacked the means to perform logical operations, and they had little text-handling capability—for example, lowercase letters were not even representable in the machines, even if there were devices capable of printing them.</p><!--[P93]--><p>These machines could be operated only by experts, and preparing a problem for computation (what would be called programming today) took a long time. With only one person at a time able to use a machine, major bottlenecks were created. Problems lined up like experiments waiting for a cyclotron or the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/space-shuttle" class="md-crosslink autoxref">space shuttle</a>. Much of the machine’s <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/precious" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="precious">precious</a> time was wasted because of this one-at-a-time <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/protocol" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="protocol">protocol</a>.</p><!--[P94]--><p>In sum, the machines were expensive and the market was still small. To be useful in a broader business market or even in a broader scientific market, computers would need application programs: word processors, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/database" class="md-crosslink autoxref">database</a> programs, and so on. These applications in turn would require programming languages in which to write them and operating systems to manage them.</p><!--[P95]--><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216052" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref723696"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer-programming-language" class="md-crosslink">Programming languages</a></h2><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216053" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">Early computer language development</h2><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216054" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref723697"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/machine-language" class="md-crosslink">Machine language</a></h2><p>One <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/implication" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="implication">implication</a> of the stored-program model was that programs could read and operate on other programs as data; that is, they would be capable of <span id="ref723699"></span>self-modification. <span id="ref723698"></span>Konrad Zuse had looked upon this possibility as “making a contract with the Devil” because of the potential for abuse, and he had chosen not to <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/implement" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="implement">implement</a> it in his machines. But self-modification was essential for achieving a true general-purpose machine.</p><!--[P96]--><p>One of the very first employments of self-modification was for computer language <span id="ref723702"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/translator-computing" class="md-crosslink">translation</a>, “language” here referring to the instructions that make the machine work. Although the earliest machines worked by flipping switches, the stored-program machines were driven by stored coded instructions, and the conventions for encoding these instructions were referred to as the machine’s language.</p><!--[P97]--><p>Writing programs for early computers meant using the machine’s language. The form of a particular machine’s language is dictated by its physical and logical structure. For example, if the machine uses registers to store intermediate results of calculations, there must be instructions for moving data between such registers.</p><!--[P98]--><p>The vocabulary and rules of <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/syntax" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="syntax">syntax</a> of machine language tend to be highly detailed and very far from the natural or mathematical language in which problems are normally formulated. The desirability of automating the translation of problems into machine language was immediately evident to users, who either had to become computer experts and programmers themselves in order to use the machines or had to rely on experts and programmers who might not fully understand the problems they were translating.</p><!--[P99]--><p>Automatic translation from pure mathematics or some other “<span id="ref723700"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/high-level-language" class="md-crosslink">high-level language</a>” to machine language was therefore necessary before computers would be useful to a broader class of users. As early as the 1830s, Charles Babbage and Lady Lovelace had recognized that such translation could be done by machine (<em>see</em> the earlier section <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref216028" class="md-crosslink">Lady Lovelace, the first programmer</a>), but they made no attempt to follow up on this idea and simply wrote their programs in machine language.</p><!--[P100]--><p><span id="ref723701"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Howard-Aiken" class="md-crosslink">Howard Aiken</a>, working in the 1930s, also saw the virtue of automated translation from a high-level language to machine language. Aiken proposed a coding machine that would be dedicated to this task, accepting high-level programs and producing the actual machine-language instructions that the computer would process.</p><!--[P101]--><p>But a separate machine was not actually necessary. The IAS model guaranteed that the stored-program computer would have the power to serve as its own coding machine. The translator program, written in machine language and running on the computer, would be fed the target program as data, and it would output machine-language instructions. This plan was altogether feasible, but the cost of the machines was so great that it was not seen as cost-effective to use them for anything that a human could do—including program translation.</p><!--[P102]--><p>Two forces, in fact, argued against the early development of high-level computer languages. One was <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/skepticism" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="skepticism">skepticism</a> that anyone outside the “priesthood” of computer operators could or would use computers directly. Consequently, early computer makers saw no need to make them more accessible to people who would not use them anyway. A second reason was <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/efficiency" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="efficiency">efficiency</a>. Any translation process would necessarily add to the computing time necessary to solve a problem, and mathematicians and operators were far cheaper by the hour than computers.</p><!--[P103]--><p>Programmers did, though, come up with specialized high-level languages, or HLLs, for computer instruction—even without automatic translators to turn their programs into machine language. They simply did the translation by hand. They did this because casting problems in an intermediate programming language, somewhere between mathematics and the highly detailed language of the machine, had the advantage of making it easier to understand the program’s logical structure and to correct, or debug, any defects in the program.</p><!--[P104]--><p>The early HLLs thus were all paper-and-pencil methods of recasting problems in an intermediate form that made it easier to write code for a machine. <span id="ref724922"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Herman-Goldstine" class="md-crosslink">Herman Goldstine</a>, with contributions from his wife, Adele Goldstine, and from John von Neumann, created a graphical representation of this process: flow diagrams. Although the diagrams were only a notational device, they were widely circulated and had great influence, evolving into what are known today as flowcharts.</p><!--[P105]--></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216055" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5">Zuse’s <span id="ref723715"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Plankalkul" class="md-crosslink">Plankalkül</a></h2><p><span id="ref723703"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Konrad-Zuse" class="md-crosslink">Konrad Zuse</a> developed the first real programming language, Plankalkül (“Plan Calculus”), in 1944–45. Zuse’s language allowed for the creation of procedures (also called routines or subroutines; stored chunks of code that could be <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/invoked" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="invoked">invoked</a> repeatedly to perform routine operations such as taking a square root) and structured data (such as a record in a database, with a mixture of alphabetic and numeric data representing, for instance, name, address, and birth date). In addition, it provided conditional statements that could modify program execution, as well as repeat, or loop, statements that would cause a marked block of statements or a subroutine to be repeated a specified number of times or for as long as some condition held.</p><!--[P106]--><p>Zuse knew that computers could do more than arithmetic, but he was aware of the <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/propensity" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="propensity">propensity</a> of anyone introduced to them to view them as nothing more than calculators. So he took pains to demonstrate nonnumeric solutions with Plankalkül. He wrote programs to check the syntactical correctness of Boolean expressions (an application in logic and text handling) and even to check chess moves.</p><!--[P107]--><p>Unlike flowcharts, Zuse’s program was no intermediate language intended for pencil-and-paper translation by mathematicians. It was deliberately intended for machine translation, and Zuse did some work toward implementing a translator for Plankalkül. He did not get very far, however; he had to disassemble his machine near the end of the war and was not able to put it back together and work on it for several years. Unfortunately, his language and his work, which were roughly a dozen years ahead of their time, were not generally known outside Germany.</p><!--[P108]--></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216056" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref723716"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/interpreter" class="md-crosslink">Interpreters</a></h2><p>HLL coding was attempted right from the start of the stored-program era in the late 1940s. <span id="ref723735"></span>Shortcode, or short-order code, was the first such language actually implemented. Suggested by John Mauchly in 1949, it was implemented by William Schmitt for the <span id="ref723755"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Binary-Automatic-Computer" class="md-crosslink">BINAC</a> computer in that year and for <span id="ref723756"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/UNIVAC" class="md-crosslink">UNIVAC</a> in 1950. Shortcode went through multiple steps: first it converted the alphabetic statements of the language to numeric codes, and then it translated these numeric codes into machine language. It was an interpreter, meaning that it translated HLL statements and executed, or performed, them one at a time—a slow process. Because of their slow execution, interpreters are now rarely used outside of program development, where they may help a programmer to locate errors quickly.</p><!--[P109]--></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216057" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref723775"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/compiler" class="md-crosslink">Compilers</a></h2><p>An <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/alternative" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="alternative">alternative</a> to this approach is what is now known as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/compiler" class="md-crosslink autoxref">compilation</a>. In <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/compilation" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="compilation">compilation</a>, the entire HLL program is converted to machine language and stored for later execution. Although translation may take many hours or even days, once the translated program is stored, it can be recalled anytime in the form of a fast-executing machine-language program.</p><!--[P110]--><p>In 1952 <span id="ref723795"></span>Heinz Rutishauser, who had worked with Zuse on his computers after the war, wrote an influential paper, “<em>Automatische Rechenplanfertigung bei programmgesteuerten Rechenmaschinen</em>” (loosely translatable as “Computer Automated Conversion of Code to Machine Language”), in which he laid down the foundations of compiler construction and described two proposed compilers. Rutishauser was later involved in creating one of the most carefully defined programming languages of this early era, ALGOL. (<em>See</em> next section, FORTRAN, COBOL, and ALGOL.)</p><!--[P111]--><p>Then, in September 1952, <span id="ref723815"></span>Alick Glennie, a student at the University of Manchester, England, created the first of several programs called <span id="ref723835"></span>Autocode for the <span id="ref723855"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Manchester-Mark-I" class="md-crosslink">Manchester Mark I</a>. Autocode was the first compiler actually to be implemented. (The language that it compiled was called by the same name.) Glennie’s compiler had little influence, however. When J. Halcombe Laning created a compiler for the <span id="ref723875"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Whirlwind-computer" class="md-crosslink">Whirlwind</a> computer at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT) two years later, he met with similar lack of interest. Both compilers had the fatal drawback of producing code that ran slower (10 times slower, in the case of Laning’s) than code handwritten in machine language.</p><!--[P112]--></section></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216058" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">FORTRAN, COBOL, and ALGOL</h2><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216059" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref723876"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Grace-Hopper" class="md-crosslink">Grace Murray Hopper</a></h2><p>While the high cost of computer resources placed a premium on fast hand-coded machine-language programs, one individual worked tirelessly to promote <span id="ref723877"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/high-level-language" class="md-crosslink">high-level programming languages</a> and their associated compilers. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Grace-Hopper" class="md-crosslink">Grace Murray Hopper</a> taught mathematics at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Vassar-College" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Vassar College</a>, Poughkeepsie, New York, from 1931 to 1943 before joining the U.S. Naval Reserve. In 1944 she was assigned to the Bureau of Ordnance Computation Project at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Harvard-University" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Harvard University</a>, where she programmed the Mark I under the direction of Howard Aiken. After World War II she joined <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/J-Presper-Eckert-Jr" class="md-crosslink">J. Presper Eckert, Jr.</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/John-Mauchly" class="md-crosslink">John Mauchly</a> at their new company and, among other things, wrote compiler software for the BINAC and UNIVAC systems. Throughout the 1950s Hopper campaigned earnestly for high-level languages across the United States, and through her public appearances she helped to remove resistance to the idea. Such urging found a receptive audience at IBM, where the management wanted to add computers to the company’s successful line of business machines.</p><!--[P113]--></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216060" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref723896"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Business-Machines-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">IBM</a> develops <span id="ref723895"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/FORTRAN" class="md-crosslink">FORTRAN</a></h2><p>In the early 1950s <span id="ref723915"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/John-Warner-Backus" class="md-crosslink">John Backus</a> convinced his managers at IBM to let him put together a team to design a language and write a compiler for it. He had a machine in mind: the <span id="ref723935"></span>IBM 704, which had built-in floating-point math operations. That the 704 used floating-point representation made it especially useful for scientific work, and Backus believed that a scientifically oriented programming language would make the machine even more attractive. Still, he understood the resistance to anything that slowed a machine down, and he set out to produce a language and a compiler that would produce code that ran virtually as fast as hand-coded machine language—and at the same time made the program-writing process a lot easier.</p><!--[P114]--><p>By 1954 Backus and a team of programmers had designed the language, which they called FORTRAN (Formula Translation). Programs written in FORTRAN looked a lot more like mathematics than machine instructions:</p><!--[P115]--><blockquote><p>DO 10 J = 1,11</p><p>I = 11 − J</p><p>Y = F(A(I + 1))</p><p>IF (400 − Y) 4,8,8</p><p>4 PRINT 5,1</p><p>5 FORMAT (I10, 10H TOO LARGE)</p></blockquote><p>The compiler was written, and the language was released with a professional-looking typeset manual (a first for programming languages) in 1957.</p><!--[P122]--><p>FORTRAN took another step toward making programming more accessible, allowing comments in the programs. The ability to insert <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/annotations" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="annotations">annotations</a>, marked to be ignored by the translator program but readable by a human, meant that a well-annotated program could be read in a certain sense by people with no programming knowledge at all. For the first time a nonprogrammer could get an idea what a program did—or at least what it was intended to do—by reading (part of) the code. It was an obvious but powerful step in opening up computers to a wider audience.</p><!--[P123]--><p>FORTRAN has continued to evolve, and it retains a large user base in academia and among scientists.</p><!--[P124]--></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216061" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref723955"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/COBOL" class="md-crosslink">COBOL</a></h2><p>About the time that Backus and his team invented FORTRAN, Hopper’s group at UNIVAC released Math-matic, a FORTRAN-like language for UNIVAC computers. It was slower than FORTRAN and not particularly successful. Another language developed at Hopper’s laboratory at the same time had more influence. <span id="ref723975"></span>Flow-matic used a more English-like syntax and vocabulary:</p><!--[P125]--><blockquote><p>1 COMPARE PART-NUMBER (A) TO PART-NUMBER (B);</p><p>IF GREATER GO TO OPERATION 13;</p><p>IF EQUAL GO TO OPERATION 4;</p><p>OTHERWISE GO TO OPERATION 2.</p></blockquote><p>Flow-matic led to the development by Hopper’s group of COBOL (Common Business-Oriented Language) in 1959. COBOL was explicitly a business programming language with a very <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/verbose" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="verbose">verbose</a> English-like style. It became central to the wide acceptance of computers by business after 1959.</p><!--[P130]--></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216062" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref723995"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/ALGOL-computer-language" class="md-crosslink">ALGOL</a></h2><p>Although both FORTRAN and COBOL were universal languages (meaning that they could, in principle, be used to solve any problem that a computer could unravel), FORTRAN was better suited for mathematicians and engineers, whereas COBOL was explicitly a business programming language.</p><!--[P131]--><p>During the late 1950s a multitude of programming languages appeared. This proliferation of incompatible specialized languages spurred an interest in the United States and Europe to create a single “<span id="ref724015"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/assembly-language" class="md-crosslink">second-generation</a>” language. A transatlantic committee soon formed to determine specifications for ALGOL (Algorithmic Language), as the new language would be called. Backus, on the American side, and Heinz Rutishauser, on the European side, were among the most influential committee members.</p><!--[P132]--><p>Although ALGOL introduced some important language ideas, it was not a commercial success. Customers preferred a known specialized language, such as FORTRAN or COBOL, to an unknown general-programming language. Only Pascal, a scientific programming-language offshoot of ALGOL, survives.</p><!--[P133]--></section></section></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216063" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref724035"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/operating-system" class="md-crosslink">Operating systems</a></h2><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216064" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724075"></span>Control programs</h2><p>In order to make the early computers truly useful and efficient, two major <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/innovations" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="innovations">innovations</a> in software were needed. One was high-level programming languages (as described in the preceding section, FORTRAN, COBOL, and ALGOL). The other was control. Today the systemwide control functions of a computer are generally subsumed under the term <em>operating system</em>, or OS. An OS handles the behind-the-scenes activities of a computer, such as orchestrating the transitions from one program to another and managing access to disk storage and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/input-output-device" class="md-crosslink autoxref">peripheral</a> devices.</p><!--[P134]--><p>The need for some kind of supervisor program was quickly recognized, but the design requirements for such a program were <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/daunting" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="daunting">daunting</a>. The supervisor program would have to run in parallel with an application program somehow, monitor its actions in some way, and seize control when necessary. Moreover, the essential—and difficult—feature of even a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/rudimentary" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="rudimentary">rudimentary</a> supervisor program was the <span id="ref724095"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/interrupt-signal" class="md-crosslink">interrupt</a> facility. It had to be able to stop a running program when necessary but save the state of the program and all registers so that after the interruption was over the program could be restarted from where it left off.</p><!--[P135]--><p>The first computer with such a true interrupt system was the UNIVAC 1103A, which had a single interrupt triggered by one fixed condition. In 1959 the Lincoln Labs TX2 generalized the interrupt capability, making it possible to set various interrupt conditions under software control. However, it would be one company, <span id="ref724115"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Business-Machines-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">IBM</a>, that would create, and dominate, a market for business computers. IBM established its primacy primarily through one invention: the IBM 360 operating system.</p><!--[P136]--></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216065" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The <span id="ref724135"></span>IBM 360</h2><p>IBM had been selling business machines since early in the century and had built Howard Aiken’s computer to his architectural specifications. But the company had been slow to implement the stored-program digital computer architecture of the early 1950s. It did develop the IBM 650, a (like UNIVAC) decimal implementation of the IAS plan—and the first computer to sell more than 1,000 units.</p><!--[P137]--><p>The invention of the <span id="ref724155"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/transistor" class="md-crosslink">transistor</a> in 1947 led IBM to reengineer its early machines from electromechanical or vacuum tube to transistor technology in the late 1950s (although the UNIVAC Model 80, delivered in 1958, was the first transistor computer). These transistorized machines are commonly referred to as <span id="ref724175"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/second-generation-computer" class="md-crosslink">second-generation computers.</a></p><!--[P138]--><p>Two IBM inventions, the magnetic disk and the high-speed chain printer, led to an expansion of the market and to the unprecedented sale of 12,000 computers of one model: the <span id="ref724195"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/IBM-1401" class="md-crosslink">IBM 1401</a>. The chain printer required a lot of magnetic core memory, and IBM engineers packaged the printer support, core memory, and disk support into the 1401, one of the first computers to use this solid-state technology.</p><!--[P139]--><p>IBM had several lines of computers developed by independent groups of engineers within the company: a scientific-technical line, a commercial data-processing line, an accounting line, a decimal machine line, and a line of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/supercomputer" class="md-crosslink">supercomputers</a>. Each line had a distinct hardware-dependent operating system, and each required separate development and maintenance of its associated application software. In the early 1960s IBM began designing a machine that would take the best of all these <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/disparate" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="disparate">disparate</a> lines, add some new technology and new ideas, and replace all the company’s computers with one single line, the 360. At an estimated development cost of $5 billion, IBM literally bet the company’s future on this new, untested architecture.</p><!--[P140]--><p>The 360 was in fact an architecture, not a single machine. Designers G.M. Amdahl, F.P. Brooks, and G.A. Blaauw explicitly separated the 360 architecture from its implementation details. The 360 architecture was intended to span a wide range of machine implementations and multiple generations of machines. The first 360 models were hybrid transistor–integrated circuit machines. <span id="ref724216"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/integrated-circuit" class="md-crosslink">Integrated circuit</a> computers are commonly referred to as <span id="ref724215"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/third-generation-computer" class="md-crosslink">third-generation computers</a>.</p><!--[P141]--><p>Key to the architecture was the operating system. OS/360 ran on all machines built to the 360 architecture—initially six machines spanning a wide range of performance characteristics and later many more machines. It had a shielded supervisory system (unlike the 1401, which could be interfered with by application programs), and it reserved certain operations as privileged in that they could be performed only by the supervisor program.</p><!--[P142]--><p>The first IBM 360 computers were delivered in 1965. The 360 architecture represented a continental divide in the relative importance of hardware and software. After the 360, computers were defined by their operating systems.</p><!--[P143]--><p>The market, on the other hand, was defined by IBM. In the late 1950s and into the 1960s, it was common to refer to the computer industry as “IBM and the Seven Dwarfs,” a reference to the relatively diminutive market share of its nearest rivals—Sperry Rand (UNIVAC), Control Data Corporation (CDC), Honeywell, Burroughs, General Electric (GE), RCA, and National Cash Register Co. During this time IBM had some 60–70 percent of all computer sales. The 360 did nothing to lessen the giant’s dominance. When the market did open up somewhat, it was not due to the efforts of, nor was it in favour of, the dwarfs. Yet, while “IBM and the Seven Dwarfs” (soon reduced to “IBM and the BUNCH of Five,” BUNCH being an <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/acronym" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="acronym">acronym</a> for Burroughs, UNIVAC, NCR, CDC, and Honeywell) continued to build Big Iron, a fundamental change was taking place in how computers were accessed.</p><!--[P144]--><ul class="assemblies medialist" data-type="other"><li><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="207544"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/94/186694-049-E77CFC99.jpg" alt="Elliott 803 computer" data-width="426" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Elliott 803 computer</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Learn about the Elliott 803, a transistorized computer made in the United Kingdom in the early 1960s.</span><cite>© Open University</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="207548"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/95/186695-049-A0FB6283.jpg" alt="ICL 2966 computer" data-width="426" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">ICL 2966 computer</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Learn about the ICL 2966, a mainframe computer utilizing integrated circuit technology, made in the United Kingdom in the 1980s.</span><cite>© Open University</cite></figcaption></figure></li></ul><!--[ML144]--></section></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216066" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref724237"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/time-sharing" class="md-crosslink">Time-sharing</a> and minicomputers</h2><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216067" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">Time-sharing from Project MAC to UNIX</h2><p>In 1959 Christopher Strachey in the United Kingdom and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/John-McCarthy" class="md-crosslink autoxref">John McCarthy</a> in the United States independently described something they called time-sharing. Meanwhile, computer pioneer <span id="ref724239"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Joseph-Carl-Robnett-Licklider" class="md-crosslink">J.C.R. Licklider</a> at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT) began to promote the idea of <span id="ref724240"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/interactive-mode" class="md-crosslink">interactive computing</a> as an alternative to batch processing. <span id="ref724238"></span>Batch processing was the normal mode of operating computers at the time: a user handed a deck of punched cards to an operator, who fed them to the machine, and an hour or more later the printed output would be made available for pickup. Licklider’s notion of interactive programming involved typing on a teletype or other keyboard and getting more or less immediate feedback from the computer on the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/teleprinter" class="md-crosslink autoxref">teletype’s</a> printer <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/mechanism-machinery" class="md-crosslink autoxref">mechanism</a> or some other output device. This was how the <span id="ref724241"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Whirlwind-computer" class="md-crosslink">Whirlwind</a> computer had been operated at MIT in 1950, and it was essentially what Strachey and McCarthy had in mind at the end of the decade.</p><!--[P145]--><p>By November 1961 a prototype time-sharing system had been produced and tested. It was built by <span id="ref724255"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Fernando-Jose-Corbato" class="md-crosslink">Fernando Corbato</a> and Robert Jano at MIT, and it connected an IBM 709 computer with three users typing away at IBM Flexowriters. This was only a prototype for a more elaborate time-sharing system that Corbato was working on, called <span id="ref724275"></span>Compatible Time-Sharing System, or CTSS. Still, Corbato was waiting for the appropriate technology to build that system. It was clear that electromechanical and vacuum tube technologies would not be adequate for the computational demands that time-sharing would place on the machines. Fast, transistor-based computers were needed.</p><!--[P146]--><p>In the meantime, Licklider had been placed in charge of a U.S. government program called the <span id="ref724295"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Defense-Advanced-Research-Projects-Agency" class="md-crosslink">Advanced Research Projects Agency</a> (ARPA), created in response to the launch of the Sputnik satellite by the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Soviet-Union" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Soviet Union</a> in 1957. ARPA researched interesting technological areas, and under Licklider’s leadership it focused on time-sharing and interactive computing. With ARPA support, CTSS evolved into <span id="ref724355"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Project-Mac" class="md-crosslink">Project MAC</a>, which went online in 1963.</p><!--[P147]--><p>Project MAC was only the beginning. Other similar time-sharing projects followed rapidly at various research institutions, and some commercial products began to be released that also were called interactive or time-sharing. (The role of ARPA in creating another time-sharing network, ARPANET, became the foundation of the Internet and is discussed in a later section, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref216094" class="md-crosslink">The Internet</a>.)</p><!--[P148]--><p>Time-sharing represented a different interaction model, and it needed a new programming language to support it. Researchers created several such languages, most notably <span id="ref724375"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/BASIC" class="md-crosslink">BASIC</a> (Beginner’s All-Purpose Symbolic Instruction Code), which was invented in 1964 at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Dartmouth-College" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Dartmouth College</a>, Hanover, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/New-Hampshire-state" class="md-crosslink autoxref">New Hampshire</a>, by <span id="ref724395"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/John-George-Kemeny" class="md-crosslink">John Kemeny</a> and <span id="ref724415"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Thomas-Kurtz" class="md-crosslink">Thomas Kurtz</a>. BASIC had features that made it ideal for time-sharing, and it was easy enough to be used by its target audience: college students. Kemeny and Kurtz wanted to open computers to a broader group of users and deliberately designed BASIC with that goal in mind. They succeeded.</p><!--[P149]--><p>Time-sharing also called for a new kind of operating system. Researchers at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/ATandT-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">AT&amp;T</a> (American Telephone and Telegraph Company) and <span id="ref724435"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/General-Electric" class="md-crosslink">GE</a> tackled the problem with funding from ARPA via Project MAC and an ambitious plan to implement time-sharing on a new computer with a new time-sharing-oriented operating system. AT&amp;T dropped out after the project was well under way, but GE went ahead, and the result was the <span id="ref724476"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Multics" class="md-crosslink">Multics</a> operating system running on the <span id="ref724436"></span>GE 645 computer. GE 645 exemplified the time-shared computer in 1965, and Multics was the model of a time-sharing operating system, built to be up seven days a week, 24 hours a day.</p><!--[P150]--><p>When AT&amp;T dropped out of the project and removed the GE machines from its laboratories, researchers at AT&amp;T’s high-tech research arm, <span id="ref724475"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Bell-Laboratories" class="md-crosslink">Bell Laboratories</a>, were upset. They felt they needed the time-sharing capabilities of Multics for their work, and so two Bell Labs workers, Ken Thompson and Dennis Ritchie, wrote their own operating system. Since the operating system was inspired by Multics but would initially be somewhat simpler, they called it <span id="ref724495"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/UNIX" class="md-crosslink">UNIX</a>.</p><!--[P151]--><p>UNIX embodied, among other innovations, the notion of pipes. Pipes allowed a user to pass the results of one program to another program for use as input. This led to a style of programming in which small, targeted, single-function programs were joined together to achieve a more complicated goal. Perhaps the most influential aspect of UNIX, though, was that Bell Labs distributed the source code (the uncompiled, human-readable form of the code that made up the operating system) freely to colleges and universities—but made no offer to support it. The freely distributed source code led to a rapid, and somewhat divergent, evolution of UNIX. Whereas initial support was attracted by its free availability, its <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/robust" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="robust">robust</a> <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/multitasking" class="md-crosslink autoxref">multitasking</a> and well-developed network security features have continued to make it the most common operating system for academic institutions and World Wide Web servers.</p><!--[P152]--></section><span id="ref216051" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216068" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724496"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/minicomputer" class="md-crosslink">Minicomputers</a></h2><p>About 1965, roughly <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/coterminous" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="coterminous">coterminous</a> with the development of time-sharing, a new kind of computer came on the scene. Small and relatively inexpensive (typically one-tenth the cost of the Big Iron machines), the new machines were stored-program computers with all the generality of the computers then in use but stripped down. The new machines were called minicomputers. (About the same time, the larger traditional computers began to be called <span id="ref724498"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/mainframe" class="md-crosslink">mainframes</a>.) Minicomputers were designed for easy connection to scientific instruments and other input/output devices, had a simplified architecture, were implemented using fast transistors, and were typically programmed in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/assembly-language" class="md-crosslink autoxref">assembly language</a> with little support for high-level languages.</p><!--[P153]--><p>Other small, inexpensive computing devices were available at the time but were not considered minicomputers. These were special-purpose scientific machines or small character-based or decimal-based machines such as the IBM 1401. They were not considered “minis,” however, because they did not meet the needs of the initial market for minis—that is, for a lab computer to control instruments and collect and analyze data.</p><!--[P154]--><p>The market for minicomputers evolved over time, but it was scientific laboratories that created the category. It was an essentially untapped market, and those manufacturers who established an early foothold dominated it. Only one of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/mainframe" class="md-crosslink autoxref">mainframe</a> manufacturers, Honeywell, was able to break into the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/minicomputer" class="md-crosslink autoxref">minicomputer</a> market in any significant way. The other main minicomputer players, such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Digital-Equipment-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Digital Equipment Corporation</a> (DEC), Data General Corporation, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Hewlett-Packard-Company" class="md-crosslink">Hewlett-Packard Company</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Texas-Instruments-Incorporated" class="md-crosslink">Texas Instruments Incorporated</a>, all came from fields outside mainframe computing, frequently from the field of electronic test equipment. The failure of the mainframe companies to gain a foothold in the minimarket may have stemmed from their failure to recognize that minis were distinct in important ways from the small computers that these companies were already making.</p><!--[P155]--><p>The first minicomputer, although it was not recognized as such at the time, may have been the MIT <span id="ref724499"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Whirlwind-computer" class="md-crosslink">Whirlwind</a> in 1950. It was designed for instrument control and had many, although not all, of the features of later minis. <span id="ref724501"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Digital-Equipment-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">DEC</a>, founded in 1957 by <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Kenneth-Olsen" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Kenneth Olsen</a> and Harlan Anderson, produced one of the first minicomputers, the <span id="ref724500"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Programmed-Data-Processor" class="md-crosslink">Programmed Data Processor</a>, or PDP-1, in 1959. At a price of $120,000, the PDP-1 sold for a fraction of the cost of mainframe computers, albeit with vastly more limited capabilities. But it was the PDP-8, using the recently invented <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/integrated-circuit" class="md-crosslink">integrated circuit</a> (a set of interconnected transistors and resistors on a single silicon wafer, or chip) and selling for around $20,000 (falling to $3,000 by the late 1970s), that was the first true mass-market minicomputer. The PDP-8 was released in 1965, the same year as the first IBM 360 machines.</p><!--[P156]--><p>The PDP-8 was the prototypical mini. It was designed to be programmed in assembly language; it was easy—physically, logically, and electrically—to attach a wide variety of input/output devices and scientific instruments to it; and it was architecturally stripped down with little support for programming—it even lacked multiplication and division operations in its initial release. It had a mere 4,096 words of memory, and its word length was 12 bits—very short even by the standards of the times. (The word is the smallest chunk of memory that a program can refer to independently; the size of the word limits the complexity of the instruction set and the efficiency of mathematical operations.) The PDP-8’s short word and small memory made it relatively underpowered for the time, but its low price more than compensated for this.</p><!--[P157]--><p>The PDP-11 shipped five years later, relaxing some of the constraints imposed on the PDP-8. It was designed to support high-level languages, had more memory and more power generally, was produced in 10 different models over 10 years, and was a great success. It was followed by the <span id="ref724502"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/VAX" class="md-crosslink">VAX</a> line, which supported an advanced operating system called <span id="ref724503"></span>VAX/VMS—VMS standing for virtual memory system, an innovation that effectively expanded the memory of the machine by allowing disk or other <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/peripheral" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="peripheral">peripheral</a> storage to serve as extra memory. By this time (the early 1970s) DEC was vying with Sperry Rand (manufacturer of the UNIVAC computer) for position as the second largest computer company in the world, though it was producing machines that had little in common with the original prototypical minis.</p><!--[P158]--><p>Although the minis’ early growth was due to their use as scientific instrument controllers and data loggers, their compelling feature turned out to be their approachability. After years of standing in line to use departmental, universitywide, or companywide machines through intermediaries, scientists and researchers could now buy their own computer and run it themselves in their own laboratories. And they had <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/intimate" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="intimate">intimate</a> access to the internals of the machine, the stripped-down architecture making it possible for a smart graduate student to reconfigure the machine to do something not intended by the manufacturer. With their own computers in their labs, researchers began to use minis for all sorts of new purposes, and the manufacturers adapted later releases of the machines to the evolving demands of the market.</p><!--[P159]--><p>The minicomputer revolution lasted about a decade. By 1975 it was coming to a close, but not because minis were becoming less attractive. The mini was about to be eclipsed by another technology: the new <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/integrated" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="integrated">integrated</a> circuits, which would soon be used to build the smallest, most affordable computers to date. The rise of this new technology is described in the next section, The personal computer revolution.</p><!--[P160]--></section></section></section><section id="ref216069" data-level="2"><h2 class="h2">The <span id="ref724505"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/personal-computer" class="md-crosslink">personal computer</a> revolution</h2><p>Before 1970, computers were big machines requiring thousands of separate transistors. They were operated by specialized technicians, who often dressed in white lab coats and were commonly referred to as a computer priesthood. The machines were expensive and difficult to use. Few people came in direct contact with them, not even their programmers. The typical interaction was as follows: a programmer coded instructions and data on preformatted paper, a keypunch operator transferred the data onto punch cards, a computer operator fed the cards into a card reader, and the computer executed the instructions or stored the cards’ information for later processing. Advanced installations might allow users limited interaction with the computer more directly, but still remotely, via time-sharing through the use of cathode-ray tube terminals or teletype machines.</p><!--[P161]--><p>At the beginning of the 1970s there were essentially two types of computers. There were room-sized mainframes, costing hundreds of thousands of dollars, that were built one at a time by companies such as IBM and CDC. There also were smaller, cheaper, mass-produced minicomputers, costing tens of thousands of dollars, that were built by a handful of companies, such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Digital-Equipment-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Digital Equipment Corporation</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Hewlett-Packard-Company" class="md-crosslink">Hewlett-Packard Company</a>, for scientific laboratories and businesses.</p><!--[P162]--><p>Still, most people had no direct contact with either type of computer, and the machines were popularly viewed as impersonal giant brains that threatened to eliminate jobs through automation. The idea that anyone would have his or her own desktop computer was generally regarded as far-fetched. Nevertheless, with advances in integrated circuit technology, the necessary building blocks for desktop computing began to emerge in the early 1970s.</p><!--[P163]--><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216070" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The <span id="ref724506"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/microprocessor" class="md-crosslink">microprocessor</a></h2><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216071" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724504"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/integrated-circuit" class="md-crosslink">Integrated circuits</a></h2><p><span id="ref724507"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/William-Shockley" class="md-crosslink">William Shockley</a>, a coinventor of the transistor, started Shockley Semiconductor Laboratories in 1955 in his hometown of Palo Alto, California. In 1957 his eight top researchers left to form <span id="ref724508"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Fairchild-Semiconductor-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Fairchild Semiconductor Corporation</a>, funded by Fairchild Camera and Instrument Corporation. Along with <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Hewlett-Packard-Company" class="md-crosslink">Hewlett-Packard</a>, another Palo Alto firm, Fairchild Semiconductor was the seed of what would become known as <span id="ref724509"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Silicon-Valley-region-California" class="md-crosslink">Silicon Valley</a>. Historically, Fairchild will always deserve recognition as one of the most important <span id="ref724510"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/semiconductor-device" class="md-crosslink">semiconductor</a> companies, having served as the training ground for most of the <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/entrepreneurs" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="entrepreneurs">entrepreneurs</a> who went on to start their own computer companies in the 1960s and early 1970s.</p><!--[P164]--><p>From the mid-1960s into the early ’70s, Fairchild Semiconductor Corporation and <span id="ref724512"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Texas-Instruments-Incorporated" class="md-crosslink">Texas Instruments Incorporated</a> were the leading manufacturers of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/integrated-circuit" class="md-crosslink">integrated circuits</a> (ICs) and were continually increasing the number of electronic components embedded in a single silicon wafer, or <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer-chip" class="md-crosslink autoxref">chip</a>. As the number of components escalated into the thousands, these chips began to be referred to as <span id="ref724511"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/large-scale-integration" class="md-crosslink">large-scale integration</a> chips, and computers using them are sometimes called <span id="ref724513"></span>fourth-generation computers. The invention of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/microprocessor" class="md-crosslink">microprocessor</a> was the culmination of this trend.</p><!--[P165]--><p>Although computers were still rare and often regarded as a threat to employment, calculators were common and accepted in offices. With advances in semiconductor technology, a market was emerging for sophisticated electronic desktop calculators. It was, in fact, a calculator project that turned into a milestone in the history of computer technology.</p><!--[P166]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216072" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The <span id="ref724516"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Intel-4004" class="md-crosslink">Intel 4004</a></h2><p>In 1969 <span id="ref724515"></span>Busicom, a Japanese calculator company, commissioned <span id="ref724519"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Intel" class="md-crosslink">Intel Corporation</a> to make the chips for a line of <span id="ref724514"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/calculator" class="md-crosslink">calculators</a> that Busicom intended to sell. Custom chips were made for many clients, and this was one more such contract, hardly unusual at the time.</p><!--[P167]--><p>Intel was one of several semiconductor companies to emerge in Silicon Valley, having spun off from Fairchild Semiconductor. Intel’s president, <span id="ref724517"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Robert-Noyce" class="md-crosslink">Robert Noyce</a>, while at Fairchild, had invented planar integrated circuits, a process in which the wiring was directly embedded in the silicon along with the electronic components at the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/manufacturing" class="md-crosslink autoxref">manufacturing</a> stage.</p><!--[P168]--><p>Intel had planned on focusing its business on memory chips, but Busicom’s request for custom chips for a calculator turned out to be a most valuable diversion. While specialized chips were effective at their given task, their small market made them expensive. Three Intel engineers—Federico Faggin, <span id="ref724518"></span>Marcian (“Ted”) Hoff, and Stan Mazor—considered the request of the Japanese firm and proposed a more versatile design.</p><!--[P169]--><p>Hoff had experience with minicomputers, which could do anything the calculator could do and more. He rebelled at building a special-purpose device when the technology existed to build a general-purpose one. The general-purpose device he had in mind, however, would be a lot like a computer, and at that time computers intimidated people while calculators did not. Moreover, there was a clear and large market for calculators and a limited one for computers—and, after all, the customer had commissioned a calculator chip.</p><!--[P170]--><p>Nevertheless, Hoff prevailed, and Intel proposed a design that was functionally very similar to a minicomputer (although not in size, power, attachable physical devices such as printers, or many other practical ways). In addition to performing the input/output functions that most ICs carried out, the design would form the instructions for the IC and would help to control, send, and receive signals from other chips and devices. A set of instructions was stored in memory, and the chip could read them and respond to them. The device would thus do everything that Busicom wanted, but it would do a lot more: it was the essence of a general-purpose computer. There was little obvious demand for such a device, but the Intel team, understanding the drawbacks of special-purpose ICs, sensed that it was an economical device that would, somehow, find a market.</p><!--[P171]--><p>At first Busicom was not interested, but Intel decided to go forward with the design anyway, and the Japanese company eventually accepted it. Intel named the chip the 4004, which referred to the number of features and transistors it had. These included memory, input/output, control, and arithmetical/logical capacities. It came to be called a microprocessor or microcomputer. It is this chip that is referred to as the brain of the personal desktop computer—the central processing unit, or CPU.</p><!--[P172]--><p>Busicom eventually sold over 100,000 calculators powered by the 4004. Busicom later also accepted a one-time payment of $60,000 that gave Intel <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/exclusive" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="exclusive">exclusive</a> rights to the 4004 design, and Intel began marketing the chip to other manufacturers in 1971.</p><!--[P173]--><p>The 4004 had significant limitations. As a four-bit processor, it was capable of only 2<sup>4</sup>, or 16, distinct combinations, or “words.” To distinguish the 26 letters of the alphabet and up to six punctuation symbols, the computer had to combine two four-bit words. Nevertheless, the 4004 achieved a level of fame when Intel found a high-profile customer for it: it was used on the <span id="ref724520"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Pioneer-space-probes" class="md-crosslink">Pioneer 10</a> space probe, launched on March 2, 1972.</p><!--[P174]--><p>It became a little easier to see the potential of microprocessors when Intel introduced an eight-bit processor, the <span id="ref724521"></span>8008, in November 1972. (In 1974 the 8008 was reengineered with a larger, more versatile instruction set as the <span id="ref724522"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Intel-8080" class="md-crosslink">8080</a>.) In 1972 Intel was still a small company, albeit with two new and revolutionary products. But no one—certainly not their inventors—had figured out exactly what to do with Intel’s microprocessors.</p><!--[P175]--><p>Intel placed in electronics magazines articles expounding the microprocessors’ capabilities and <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/proselytized" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="proselytized">proselytized</a> engineering organizations and companies in the hope that others would come up with applications. With the basic capabilities of a computer now available on a tiny speck of silicon, some observers realized that this was the dawn of a new age of computing. That new age would centre on the microcomputer.</p><!--[P176]--></section></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216073" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The <span id="ref724523"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/microcomputer" class="md-crosslink">microcomputer</a></h2><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216074" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">Early computer enthusiasts</h2><p>Though the young engineering executives at Intel could sense the ground shifting upon the introduction of their new microprocessors, the leading computer manufacturers did not. It should not have taken a visionary to observe the trend of cheaper, faster, and more powerful devices. Nevertheless, even after the invention of the microprocessor, few could imagine a market for personal computers.</p><!--[P177]--><p>The advent of the microprocessor did not inspire IBM or any other large company to begin producing personal computers. Time after time, the big computer companies overlooked the opportunity to bring computing capabilities to a much broader market. In some cases, they turned down explicit proposals by their own engineers to build such machines. Instead, the new generation of microcomputers or personal computers emerged from the minds and passions of electronics hobbyists and entrepreneurs.</p><!--[P178]--><p>In the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/San-Francisco-Bay" class="md-crosslink autoxref">San Francisco Bay</a> area, the advances of the semiconductor industry were gaining recognition and stimulating a grassroots computer movement. Lee Felsenstein, an electronics engineer active in the student antiwar movement of the 1960s, started an organization called <span id="ref724524"></span>Community Memory to install computer terminals in storefronts. This movement was a sign of the times, an attempt by computer cognoscenti to empower the masses by giving ordinary individuals access to a public computer network.</p><!--[P179]--><p>The frustration felt by engineers and electronics hobbyists who wanted easier access to computers was expressed in articles in the electronics magazines in the early 1970s. Magazines such as <em>Popular Electronics</em> and <em>Radio Electronics</em> helped spread the notion of a personal computer.
