And in the San Francisco Bay area and elsewhere hobbyists organized computer clubs to discuss how to build their own computers.</p><!--[P180]--><p><span id="ref724525"></span>Dennis Allison wrote a version of <span id="ref724526"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/BASIC" class="md-crosslink">BASIC</a> for these early personal computers and, with Bob Albrecht, published the code in 1975 in a newsletter called <em>Dr. Dobb’s Journal of Computer Calisthenics and Orthodontia</em>, later changed to <em><span id="ref724527"></span>Dr. Dobb’s Journal</em>. <em>Dr. Dobb’s</em> is still publishing programming tips and public domain software, making programs available to anyone willing to type them into a computer. The publication continues to reflect the early passion for sharing computer knowledge and software.</p><!--[P181]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216075" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The <span id="ref724528"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Altair-computer" class="md-crosslink">Altair</a></h2><p>In September 1973 <em>Radio Electronics</em> published an article describing a “<span id="ref724529"></span>TV Typewriter,” which was a computer terminal that could connect a hobbyist with a mainframe computer. It was written by Don Lancaster, an aerospace engineer and fire spotter in Arizona who was also a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/prolific" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="prolific">prolific</a> author of do-it-yourself articles for electronics hobbyists. The TV Typewriter provided the first display of alphanumeric information on a common television set. It influenced a generation of computer hobbyists to start thinking about real “home-brewed” computers.</p><!--[P182]--><p>The next step was the personal computer itself. That same year a French company, R2E, developed the <span id="ref724531"></span>Micral microcomputer using the 8008 processor. The Micral was the first commercial, non-kit microcomputer. Although the company sold 500 Micrals in France that year, it was little known among American hobbyists.</p><!--[P183]--><p>Instead, a company called <span id="ref724532"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Micro-Instrumentation-and-Telemetry-Systems" class="md-crosslink">Micro Instrumentation Telemetry Systems</a>, which rapidly became known as MITS, made the big American splash. This company, located in a tiny office in an Albuquerque, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/New-Mexico" class="md-crosslink autoxref">New Mexico</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/shopping-centre" class="md-crosslink autoxref">shopping centre</a>, had started out selling radio transmitters for model airplanes in 1968. It expanded into the kit calculator business in the early 1970s. This move was terribly ill-timed because other, larger manufacturers such as Hewlett-Packard and Texas Instruments (itself a leading designer of ICs) soon moved into the market with mass-produced <span id="ref724533"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/calculator" class="md-crosslink">calculators</a>. As a result, calculators quickly became smaller, more powerful, and cheaper. By 1974 the average cost for a calculator had dropped from several hundred dollars to about $25, and MITS was on the verge of bankruptcy.</p><!--[P184]--><p>In need of a new product, MITS came up with the idea of selling a computer kit. The kit, containing all of the components necessary to build an Altair computer, sold for $397, barely more than the list cost of the Intel 8080 microprocessor that it used. A January 1975 cover article in <em>Popular Electronics</em> generated hundreds of orders for the kit, and MITS was saved.</p><!--[P185]--><p>The firm did its best to live up to its promise of delivery within 60 days, and to do so it limited manufacture to a bare-bones kit that included a box, a CPU board with 256 bytes of memory, and a front panel. The machines, especially the early ones, had only limited reliability. To make them work required many hours of assembly by an electronics expert.</p><!--[P186]--><p>When assembled, Altairs were blue, box-shaped machines that measured 17 inches by 18 inches by 7 inches (approximately 43 cm by 46 cm by 18 cm). There was no keyboard, video terminal, paper-tape reader, or printer. There was no software. All programming was in assembly language. The only way to input programs was by setting switches on the front panel for each instruction, step-by-step. A pattern of flashing lights on the front panel indicated the results of a program.</p><!--[P187]--><p>Just getting the Altair to blink its lights represented an accomplishment. Nevertheless, it sparked people’s interest. In Silicon Valley, members of a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/nascent" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="nascent">nascent</a> hobbyist group called the <span id="ref724534"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Homebrew-Computer-Club" class="md-crosslink">Homebrew Computer Club</a> gathered around an Altair at one of their first meetings. Homebrew epitomized the passion and antiestablishment <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/camaraderie" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="camaraderie">camaraderie</a> that characterized the hobbyist <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/community" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="community">community</a> in Silicon Valley. At their meetings, chaired by Felsenstein, attendees compared digital devices that they were constructing and discussed the latest articles in electronics magazines.</p><!--[P188]--><p>In one important way, MITS modeled the Altair after the minicomputer. It had a bus structure, a data path for sending instructions throughout its circuitry that would allow it to house and communicate with <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/plug-in" class="md-crosslink autoxref">add-on</a> circuit boards. The Altair hardly represented a singular revolutionary invention, along the lines of the transistor, but it did encourage sweeping change, giving hobbyists the confidence to take the next step.</p><!--[P189]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216076" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The hobby market expands</h2><p>Some entrepreneurs, particularly in the San Francisco Bay area, saw opportunities to build add-on devices, or <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/peripherals" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="peripherals">peripherals</a>, for the Altair; others decided to design competitive hardware products. Because different machines might use different data paths, or buses, peripherals built for one computer might not work with another computer. This led the emerging industry to petition the Institute for Electrical and Electronics Engineers to select a standard <span id="ref724535"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/bus-computer-science" class="md-crosslink">bus</a>. The resulting standard, the S-100 bus, was open for all to use and became <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/ubiquitous" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="ubiquitous">ubiquitous</a> among early personal computers. Standardizing on a common bus helped to expand the market for early peripheral manufacturers, spurred the development of new devices, and relieved computer manufacturers of the onerous need to develop their own <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/proprietary" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="proprietary">proprietary</a> peripherals.</p><!--[P190]--><p>These early microcomputer companies took the first steps toward building a personal computer industry, but most of them eventually collapsed, unable to build enough reliable machines or to offer sufficient customer support. In general, most of the early companies lacked the proper <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/balance-measuring-instrument" class="md-crosslink autoxref">balance</a> of engineers, entrepreneurs, capital, and marketing experience. But perhaps even more significant was a dearth of software that could make personal computers useful to a larger, nonhobbyist market.</p><!--[P191]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216077" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">Early microcomputer <span id="ref724536"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/software" class="md-crosslink">software</a></h2><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216078" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5">From <em>Star Trek</em> to Microsoft</h2><p>The first programs developed for the hobbyists’ microcomputers were <span id="ref724537"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/electronic-game" class="md-crosslink">games</a>. With the early machines limited in graphic capabilities, most of these were text-based adventure or role-playing games. However, there were a few graphical games, such as <em>Star Trek</em>, which were popular on mainframes and minicomputers and were converted to run on microcomputers. One company created the game <em>Micro Chess</em> and used the profits to fund the development of an important program called <span id="ref724538"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/VisiCalc" class="md-crosslink">VisiCalc</a>, the industry’s first <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/spreadsheet" class="md-crosslink autoxref">spreadsheet</a> software. These games, in addition to demonstrating some of the microcomputer’s capabilities, helped to convince ordinary individuals, in particular small-business owners, that they could operate a computer.</p><!--[P192]--><p>As was the case with large computers, the creation of application software for the machines waited for the development of programming languages and operating systems. <span id="ref724539"></span>Gary Kildall developed the first operating system for a microcomputer as part of a project he contracted with Intel several years before the release of the Altair. Kildall realized that a computer had to be able to handle storage devices such as disk drives, and for this purpose he developed an operating system called <span id="ref724540"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/CP-M" class="md-crosslink">CP/M</a>.</p><!--[P193]--><p>There was no obvious use for such software at the time, and Intel agreed that Kildall could keep it. Later, when a few microcomputer companies had emerged from among the hobbyists and entrepreneurs inspired by MITS, a company called IMSAI realized that an operating system would attract more software to its machine, and it chose CP/M. Most companies followed suit, and Kildall’s company, Digital Research, became one of the first software giants in the emerging microcomputer industry.</p><!--[P194]--><p><span id="ref724541"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/high-level-language" class="md-crosslink">High-level languages</a> were also needed in order for programmers to develop applications. Two young programmers realized this almost immediately upon hearing of the MITS Altair. Childhood friends <span id="ref724542"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Bill-Gates" class="md-crosslink">William (“Bill”) Gates</a> and <span id="ref724545"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Paul-Allen" class="md-crosslink">Paul Allen</a> were whiz kids with computers as they grew up in Seattle, Washington, debugging software on minicomputers at the ages of 13 and 15, respectively. As teenagers they had started a company and had built the hardware and written the software that would provide statistics on traffic flow from a rubber tube strung across a highway. Later, when the Altair came out, Allen quit his job, and Gates left Harvard University, where he was a student, in order to create a version of the programming language BASIC that could run on the new computer. They licensed their version of BASIC to MITS and started calling their partnership <span id="ref724546"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Microsoft-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Microsoft</a>. The Microsoft Corporation went on to develop versions of BASIC for nearly every computer that was released. It also developed other high-level languages. When IBM eventually decided to enter the microcomputer business in 1980, it called on Microsoft for both a programming language and an operating system, and the small partnership was on its way to becoming the largest software company in the world. (<em>See</em> the section <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref235881" class="md-crosslink">The IBM Personal Computer</a>.)</p><!--[P195]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216079" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5">Application software</h2><p>The availability of BASIC and CP/M enabled more widespread software development. By 1977 a two-person firm called <span id="ref724547"></span>Structured Systems Group started developing a <span id="ref724548"></span>General Ledger program, perhaps the first serious business software, which sold for $995. The company shipped its software in ziplock bags with a manual, a practice that became common in the industry. General Ledger began to familiarize business managers with microcomputers. Another important program was the first microcomputer <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/word-processor" class="md-crosslink autoxref">word processor</a>, called <span id="ref724549"></span>Electric Pencil, developed by a former camera operator turned computer hobbyist. Electric Pencil was one of the first programs that allowed nontechnical people to perform useful tasks on personal computers. Nevertheless, the early personal computer companies still underestimated the value of software, and many refused to pay the software developer to convert Electric Pencil to run on their machines. Eventually the availability of some software would play a major role in determining the success of a computer.</p><!--[P196]--><p>In 1979 a Harvard business graduate named Dan Bricklin and a programmer named Bob Frankston developed <span id="ref724550"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/VisiCalc" class="md-crosslink">VisiCalc</a>, the first personal computer financial analysis tool. VisiCalc made business forecasting much simpler, allowing individuals to ask “What if” questions about numerical data and get the sort of immediate response that was not even possible for giant corporations using mainframe computer systems. Personal Software, the company that distributed VisiCalc, became hugely successful. With a few companies such as Microsoft leading the way, a software industry separate from the hardware field began to emerge.</p><!--[P197]--></section></section></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235877" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The <span id="ref724551"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/personal-computer" class="md-crosslink">personal computer</a></h2><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235878" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724552"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Commodore-Business-Machines" class="md-crosslink">Commodore</a> and Tandy enter the field</h2><p>In late 1976 Commodore Business Machines, an established electronics firm that had been active in producing electronic calculators, bought a small hobby-computer company named MOS Technology. For the first time, an established company with extensive distribution channels would be selling a microcomputer.</p><!--[P198]--><p>The next year, another established company entered the microcomputer market. <span id="ref724553"></span>Tandy Corporation, best known for its chain of Radio Shack stores, had followed the development of MITS and decided to enter the market with its own <span id="ref724554"></span>TRS-80 microcomputer, which came with four kilobytes of memory, a Z80 microprocessor, a BASIC programming language, and cassettes for data storage. To cut costs, the machine was built without the ability to type lowercase letters. Thanks to Tandy’s chain of stores and the breakthrough price ($399 fully assembled and tested), the machine was successful enough to convince the company to introduce a more powerful computer two years later, the TRS-80 Model II, which could reasonably be marketed as a small-business computer. Tandy started selling its computers in greater volumes than most of the microcomputer start-ups, except for one.</p><!--[P199]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="207552"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/96/186696-049-B7BB17DB.jpg" alt="BBC Micro" data-width="426" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">BBC Micro</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Learn about the BBC Micro, a microcomputer made in the 1980s by Acorn Computers, a British company.</span><cite>© Open University</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML199]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235879" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724555"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Apple-Inc" class="md-crosslink">Apple Inc.</a></h2><p>Like the founding of the early chip companies and the invention of the microprocessor, the story of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Apple-Inc" class="md-crosslink">Apple</a> is a key part of Silicon Valley folklore. Two whiz kids, <span id="ref724556"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Stephen-Gary-Wozniak" class="md-crosslink">Stephen G. Wozniak</a> and <span id="ref724557"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Steve-Jobs" class="md-crosslink">Steven P. Jobs</a>, shared an interest in electronics. Wozniak was an early and regular participant at <span id="ref724558"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Homebrew-Computer-Club" class="md-crosslink">Homebrew Computer Club</a> meetings (<em>see</em> the earlier section, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref216075" class="md-crosslink">The Altair</a>), which Jobs also occasionally attended.</p><!--[P200]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="19609"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/02/24402-004-85152716.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/02/24402-004-85152716.jpg" alt="The Apple ISteven Jobs (right) and Stephen Wozniak holding an Apple I circuit board, c. 1976." data-width="450" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The Apple ISteven Jobs (right) and Stephen Wozniak holding an Apple I circuit board, <em>c.</em> 1976.</span><cite>Courtesy of Apple Computer, Inc.</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML200]--><p>Wozniak purchased one of the early microprocessors, the Mostek 6502 (made by MOS Technology), and used it to design a <span id="ref724755"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Apple-I" class="md-crosslink">computer</a>. When Hewlett-Packard, where he had an internship, declined to build his design, he shared his progress at a Homebrew meeting, where Jobs suggested that they could sell it together. Their initial plans were modest. Jobs figured that they could sell it for $50, twice what the parts cost them, and that they could sell hundreds of them to hobbyists. The product was actually only a <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/printed-circuit" class="md-crosslink autoxref">printed circuit</a> board. It lacked a case, a keyboard, and a power supply. Jobs got an order for 50 of the machines from Paul Terrell, owner of one of the industry’s first computer retail stores and a frequent Homebrew attendee. To raise the capital to buy the parts they needed, Jobs sold his minibus and Wozniak his calculator. They met their 30-day deadline and continued production in Jobs’s parents’ garage.</p><!--[P201]--><p>After their initial success, Jobs sought out the kind of help that other industry pioneers had shunned. While he and Wozniak began work on the <span id="ref724559"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Apple-II" class="md-crosslink">Apple II</a>, he consulted with a venture capitalist and enlisted an advertising company to aid him in marketing. As a result, in late 1976 A.C. (“Mike”) Markkula, a retired semiconductor company executive, helped write a business plan for Apple, lined up credit from a bank, and hired a serious businessman to run the venture. Apple was clearly taking a different path from its competitors. For instance, while Altair and the other microcomputer start-ups ran advertisements in technical journals, Apple ran an early colour ad in <em>Playboy</em> magazine. Its executive team lined up nationwide distributors. Apple made sure each of its subsequent products featured an elegant, consumer-style design. It also published well-written and carefully designed manuals to instruct consumers on the use of the machines. Other manuals explained all the technical details any third-party hardware or software company would have to know to build peripherals. In addition, Apple quickly built well-engineered products that made the Apple II far more useful: a printer card, a serial card, a communications card, a memory card, and a <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/floppy-disk" class="md-crosslink autoxref">floppy disk</a>. This distinctive approach <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/resonated" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="resonated">resonated</a> well in the marketplace.</p><!--[P202]--><p>In 1980 the <span id="ref724560"></span>Apple III was introduced. For this new computer Apple designed a new operating system, though it also offered a capability known as emulation that allowed the machine to run the same software, albeit much slower, as the Apple II. After several months on the market the Apple III was recalled so that certain defects could be repaired (proving that Apple was not immune to the technical failures from which most early firms suffered), but upon reintroduction to the marketplace it never achieved the success of its predecessor (demonstrating how difficult it can be for a company to introduce a computer that is not completely compatible with its existing product line).</p><!--[P203]--><p>Nevertheless, the flagship Apple II and successors in that line—the Apple II+, the Apple IIe, and the Apple IIc—made Apple into the leading personal computer company in the world. In 1980 it announced its first public stock offering, and its young founders became instant millionaires. After three years in business, Apple’s revenues had increased from $7.8 million to $117.9 million.</p><!--[P204]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235880" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The <span id="ref724561"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/graphical-user-interface" class="md-crosslink">graphical user interface</a></h2><p>In 1982 Apple introduced its <span id="ref724562"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Lisa" class="md-crosslink">Lisa</a> computer, a much more powerful computer with many innovations. The Lisa used a more advanced microprocessor, the Motorola 68000. It also had a different way of interacting with the user, called a <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/graphical-user-interface" class="md-crosslink">graphical user interface</a> (GUI). The GUI replaced the typed command lines common on previous computers with graphical icons on the screen that invoked actions when pointed to by a handheld pointing device called the mouse. The Lisa was not successful, but Apple was already preparing a scaled-down, lower-cost version called the <span id="ref724563"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Macintosh" class="md-crosslink">Macintosh</a>. Introduced in 1984, the Macintosh became wildly successful and, by making desktop computers easier to use, further popularized personal computers.</p><!--[P205]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="19724"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/82/27082-004-1EFDACB3.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/82/27082-004-1EFDACB3.jpg" alt="The first graphical user interfaceThe Xerox Alto was the first computer to use graphical icons and a mouse to control the system." data-width="384" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The first graphical user interfaceThe Xerox Alto was the first computer to use graphical icons and a mouse to control the system.</span><cite>Courtesy of Xerox</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML205]--><p>The Lisa and the Macintosh popularized several ideas that originated at other research laboratories in Silicon Valley and elsewhere. These underlying <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/intellectual" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="intellectual">intellectual</a> ideas, centred on the potential impact that computers could have on people, had been nurtured first by <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Vannevar-Bush" class="md-crosslink">Vannevar Bush</a> in the 1940s and then by <span id="ref724564"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Douglas-Engelbart" class="md-crosslink">Douglas Engelbart</a>. Like Bush, who inspired him, Engelbart was a visionary. As early as 1963 he was predicting that the computer would eventually become a tool to augment human intellect, and he specifically described many of the uses computers would have, such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/word-processing" class="md-crosslink autoxref">word processing</a>. In 1968, as a researcher at the Stanford Research Institute (SRI), Engelbart gave a remarkable demonstration of the “<span id="ref724566"></span>NLS” (o<em>NL</em>ine <em>S</em>ystem), which featured a keyboard and a <span id="ref724565"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/mouse-computer-device" class="md-crosslink">mouse</a>, a device he had invented that was used to select commands from a menu of choices shown on a display screen. The screen was divided into multiple windows, each able to display text—a single line or an entire document—or an image. Today almost every popular computer comes with a mouse and features a system that utilizes windows on the display. (<em>See</em> <span data-assembly-id="50966" class="md-link media-overlay-link">photograph</span>.)</p><!--[P206]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="50966"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/44/27144-004-822DD3F9.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/44/27144-004-822DD3F9.jpg" alt="The first computer mouseDouglas Engelbart invented the computer mouse in 1963–64 as part of an experiment to find a better way to point and click on a display screen. Fashioned at the Stanford Research Institute, it had a carved wood casing and just one button. A subsequent model had three buttons, and Engelbart would have provided more if there had been room for more than the three microswitches to which the buttons were connected." data-width="470" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The first computer mouseDouglas Engelbart invented the computer mouse in 1963–64 as part of an experiment to find a better way to point and click on a display screen. Fashioned at the Stanford Research Institute, it had a carved wood casing and just one button. A subsequent model had three buttons, and Engelbart would have provided more if there had been room for more than the three microswitches to which the buttons were connected.</span><cite>Courtesy of the Bootstrap Institute</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML206]--><p>In the 1970s some of Engelbart’s colleagues left SRI for Xerox Corporation’s <span id="ref724568"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Xerox-PARC" class="md-crosslink">Palo Alto (California) Research Center</a> (PARC), which became a hotbed of computer research. In the coming years scientists at PARC pioneered many new technologies. Xerox built a prototype computer with a GUI operating system called the <span id="ref724569"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Alto-computer" class="md-crosslink">Alto</a> and eventually introduced a commercial version called the Xerox Star in 1981. Xerox’s efforts to market this computer were a failure, and the company withdrew from the market. Apple with its Lisa and Macintosh computers and then Microsoft with its Windows operating system imitated the design of the Alto and Star systems in many ways.</p><!--[P207]--><p>Two computer scientists at PARC, <span id="ref724571"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Alan-Kay" class="md-crosslink">Alan Kay</a> and <span id="ref724570"></span>Adele Goldberg, published a paper in the early 1970s describing a vision of a powerful and portable computer they dubbed the <span id="ref724572"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Dynabook" class="md-crosslink">Dynabook</a>. The <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/prototypes" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="prototypes">prototypes</a> of this machine were expensive and resembled sewing machines, but the vision of the two researchers greatly influenced the evolution of products that today are dubbed notebook or laptop computers.</p><!--[P208]--><p>Another researcher at PARC, <span id="ref724573"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Robert-Metcalfe" class="md-crosslink">Robert Metcalfe</a>, developed a network system in 1973 that could transmit and receive data at three million bits a second, much faster than was generally thought possible at the time. Xerox did not see this as related to its core business of copiers, and it allowed Metcalfe to start his own company based on the system, called <span id="ref724574"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Ethernet" class="md-crosslink">Ethernet</a>. Ethernet eventually became the technical standard for connecting digital computers together in an office environment.</p><!--[P209]--><p>PARC researchers used Ethernet to connect their Altos together and to share another invention of theirs, the laser printer. Laser printers work by shooting a stream of light that gives a charge to the surface of a rotating drum. The charged area attracts toner powder so that when paper rolls over it an image is transferred. PARC programmers also developed numerous other innovations, such as the Smalltalk programming language, designed to make programming accessible to users who were not computer experts, and a text editor called Bravo, which displayed text on a computer screen exactly as it would look on paper.</p><!--[P210]--><p>Xerox PARC came up with these innovations but left it to others to commercialize them. Today they are viewed as commonplace.</p><!--[P211]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235881" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The IBM Personal Computer</h2><p>The entry of <span id="ref724575"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Business-Machines-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">IBM</a> did more to legitimize personal computers than any event in the industry’s history. By 1980 the personal computer field was starting to interest the large computer companies. <span id="ref724576"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Hewlett-Packard-Company" class="md-crosslink">Hewlett-Packard</a>, which had earlier turned down Stephen G. Wozniak’s proposal to enter the personal computer field, was now ready to enter this business, and in January 1980 it brought out its <span id="ref724577"></span>HP-85. Hewlett-Packard’s machine was more expensive ($3,250) than those of most competitors, and it used a cassette <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/belt-drive" class="md-crosslink autoxref">tape drive</a> for storage while most companies were already using disk drives. Another problem was its closed architecture, which made it difficult for third parties to develop applications or software for it.</p><!--[P212]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="100912"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/08/23608-004-47D6DC21.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/08/23608-004-47D6DC21.jpg" alt="The IBM Personal Computer (PC) was introduced in 1981. Microsoft supplied the machine's operating system, MS-DOS (Microsoft Disk Operating System)." data-width="500" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The IBM Personal Computer (PC) was introduced in 1981. Microsoft supplied the machine's operating system, MS-DOS (Microsoft Disk Operating System).</span><cite>IBM Archives</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML212]--><p>Throughout its history IBM had shown a willingness to place bets on new technologies, such as the 360 architecture. (<em>See</em> the earlier section <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref216065" class="md-crosslink">The IBM 360</a>.) Its long-term success was due largely to its ability to innovate and to adapt its business to technological change. “Big Blue,” as the company was commonly known, introduced the first computer disk storage system, the <span id="ref724595"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/RAMAC" class="md-crosslink">RAMAC</a>, which showed off its capabilities by answering world history questions in 10 languages at the 1958 World’s Fair. From 1956 to 1971 IBM sales had grown from $900 million to $8 billion, and its number of employees had increased from 72,500 to 270,000. IBM had also innovated new marketing techniques such as the unbundling of hardware, software, and computer services. So it was not a surprise that IBM would enter the fledgling but promising personal computer business.</p><!--[P213]--><p>In fact, right from project <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/conception" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="conception">conception</a>, IBM took an intelligent approach to the personal computer field. It noticed that the market for personal computers was spreading rapidly among both businesses and individuals. To move more rapidly than usual, IBM recruited a team of 12 engineers to build a prototype computer. Once the project was approved, IBM picked another small team of engineers to work on the project at its <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Boca-Raton" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Boca Raton</a>, Florida, laboratories. Philip Estridge, manager of the project, owned an Apple II and appreciated its open architecture, which allowed for the easy development of add-on products. IBM contracted with other companies to produce components for their computer and to base it on an open architecture that could be built with commercially available materials. With this plan, IBM would be able to avoid corporate bottlenecks and bring its computer to market in a year, more rapidly than competitors. Intel Corporation’s 16-bit 8088 microprocessor was selected as the central processing unit (CPU) for the computer, and for software IBM turned to <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Microsoft-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Microsoft Corporation</a>. Until then the small software company had concentrated mostly on computer languages, but <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Bill-Gates" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Bill Gates</a> and Paul Allen found it impossible to turn down this opportunity. They purchased a small operating system from another company and turned it into PC-DOS (or MS-DOS, or sometimes just <span id="ref724615"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/MS-DOS" class="md-crosslink">DOS</a>, for disk operating system), which quickly became the standard operating system for the IBM Personal Computer. IBM had first approached Digital Research to inquire about its <span id="ref724635"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/CP-M" class="md-crosslink">CP/M</a> operating system, but Digital’s executives balked at signing IBM’s nondisclosure agreement. Later IBM also offered a version of CP/M but priced it higher than DOS, sealing the fate of the operating system. In reality, DOS resembled CP/M in both <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/function-mathematics" class="md-crosslink autoxref">function</a> and appearance, and users of CP/M found it easy to convert to the new IBM machines.</p><!--[P214]--><p>IBM had the benefit of its own experience to know that <span id="ref724636"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/software" class="md-crosslink">software</a> was needed to make a computer useful. In preparation for the release of its computer, IBM contracted with several software companies to develop important applications. From day one it made available a word processor, a spreadsheet program, and a series of business programs. Personal computers were just starting to gain acceptance in businesses, and in this market IBM had a built-in advantage, as expressed in the <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/adage" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="adage">adage</a> “Nobody was ever fired for buying from IBM.”</p><!--[P215]--><p>IBM named its product the IBM Personal Computer, which quickly was shortened to the <span id="ref724637"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/IBM-PC" class="md-crosslink">IBM PC</a>. It was an immediate success, selling more than 500,000 units in its first two years. More powerful than other desktop computers at the time, it came with 16 kilobytes of memory (expandable to 256 kilobytes), one or two floppy disk drives, and an optional colour monitor. The giant company also took an unlikely but wise marketing approach by selling the IBM PC through computer dealers and in department stores, something it had never done before.</p><!--[P216]--><p>IBM’s entry into personal computers broadened the market and energized the industry. Software developers, aware of Big Blue’s immense resources and anticipating that the PC would be successful, set out to write programs for the computer. Even competitors benefited from the attention that IBM brought to the field; and when they realized that they could build machines compatible with the IBM PC, the industry rapidly changed.</p><!--[P217]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235882" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The market expands</h2><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235883" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref724639"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/clone-computer-science" class="md-crosslink">PC clones</a></h2><p>In 1982 a well-funded start-up firm called <span id="ref724638"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Compaq-Computer-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Compaq Computer Corporation</a> came out with a portable computer that was compatible with the IBM PC. These first portables resembled sewing machines when they were closed and weighed about 28 pounds (approximately 13 kg)—at the time a true lightweight. Compatibility with the IBM PC meant that any software or peripherals, such as printers, developed for use with the IBM PC would also work on the Compaq portable. The machine caught IBM by surprise and was an immediate success. Compaq was not only successful but showed other firms how to compete with IBM. Quickly thereafter many computer firms began offering “PC clones.” IBM’s decision to use off-the-shelf parts, which once seemed brilliant, had altered the company’s ability to control the computer industry as it always had with previous generations of technology.</p><!--[P218]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="19722"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/80/27080-004-3D09DDD7.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/80/27080-004-3D09DDD7.jpg" alt="The Compaq portable computerCompaq Computer Corporation introduced the first IBM-compatible portable computer in November 1982. At a weight of about 25 pounds (11 kilograms), it was sometimes referred to as a “luggable” computer." data-width="457" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The Compaq portable computerCompaq Computer Corporation introduced the first IBM-compatible portable computer in November 1982. At a weight of about 25 pounds (11 kilograms), it was sometimes referred to as a “luggable” computer.</span><cite>Courtesy of Compaq Computer Corp.</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML218]--><p>The change also hurt <span id="ref724640"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Apple-Inc" class="md-crosslink">Apple</a>, which found itself isolated as the only company not sharing in the standard PC design. Apple’s Macintosh was successful, but it could never hope to attract the customer base of all the companies building IBM PC compatibles. Eventually software companies began to favour the PC makers with more of their development efforts, and Apple’s market share began to drop. Apple cofounder Stephen Wozniak left in February 1985 to become a teacher, and Apple cofounder Steven Jobs was ousted in a power struggle in September 1985. During the ensuing turmoil, Apple held on to its loyal customer base, thanks to its innovative user interface and overall ease of use, but its market share continued to erode as lower-costing PCs began to catch up with, and even pass, Apple’s technological lead.</p><!--[P219]--></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235884" data-level="5"><h2 class="h5"><span id="ref724642"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Microsoft-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Microsoft</a>’s <span id="ref724641"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Windows-OS" class="md-crosslink">Windows</a> operating system</h2><p>In 1985 Microsoft came out with its Windows operating system, which gave PC compatibles some of the same capabilities as the Macintosh. Year after year, Microsoft refined and improved Windows so that Apple, which failed to come up with a significant new advantage, lost its edge. IBM tried to establish yet another operating system, OS/2, but lost the battle to Gates’s company. In fact, Microsoft also had established itself as the leading provider of application software for the Macintosh. Thus Microsoft dominated not only the operating system and application software business for PC-compatibles but also the application software business for the only nonstandard system with any sizable share of the desktop computer market. In 1998, amid a growing chorus of complaints about Microsoft’s business tactics, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/US-Department-of-Justice" class="md-crosslink autoxref">U.S. Department of Justice</a> filed a lawsuit charging Microsoft with using its monopoly position to stifle competition.</p><!--[P220]--></section></section><span id="ref216069" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref235885" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724643"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/workstation" class="md-crosslink">Workstation</a> computers</h2><p>While the personal computer market grew and matured, a variation on its theme grew out of university labs and began to threaten the minicomputers for their market. The new machines were called workstations. They looked like personal computers, and they sat on a single desktop and were used by a single individual just like personal computers, but they were distinguished by being more powerful and expensive, by having more complex architectures that spread the computational load over more than one CPU chip, by usually running the UNIX operating system, and by being targeted to scientists and engineers, software and chip designers, graphic artists, moviemakers, and others needing high performance. Workstations existed in a narrow <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/niche" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="niche">niche</a> between the cheapest minicomputers and the most powerful personal computers, and each year they had to become more powerful, pushing at the minicomputers even as they were pushed at by the high-end personal computers.</p><!--[P221]--><p>The most successful of the workstation manufacturers were <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Sun-Microsystems-Inc" class="md-crosslink">Sun Microsystems</a>, Inc., started by people involved in <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/enhancing" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="enhancing">enhancing</a> the UNIX operating system, and, for a time, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/SGI" class="md-crosslink">Silicon Graphics</a>, Inc., which marketed machines for video and audio editing.</p><!--[P222]--><p>The microcomputer market now included personal computers, software, peripheral devices, and workstations. Within two decades this market had surpassed the market for mainframes and minicomputers in sales and every other measure. As if to underscore such growth, in 1996 <span id="ref724655"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/SGI" class="md-crosslink">Silicon Graphics</a>, a workstation manufacturer, bought the star of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/supercomputer" class="md-crosslink">supercomputer</a> manufacturers, Cray Research, and began to develop supercomputers as a sideline. Moreover, Compaq Computer Corporation—which had parlayed its success with portable PCs into a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/perennial" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="perennial">perennial</a> position during the 1990s as the leading seller of microcomputers—bought the reigning king of the minicomputer manufacturers, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Digital-Equipment-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">Digital Equipment Corporation</a> (DEC). Compaq announced that it intended to fold DEC technology into its own expanding product line and that the DEC <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/trademark" class="md-crosslink autoxref">brand name</a> would be gradually phased out. Microcomputers were not only outselling mainframes and minis, they were blotting them out.</p><!--[P223]--></section></section></section><section id="ref216089" data-level="2"><h2 class="h2">Living in cyberspace</h2><span id="ref216089" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216090" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">Ever smaller computers</h2><span id="ref216089" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216091" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724675"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/embedded-processor" class="md-crosslink">Embedded systems</a></h2><p>One can look at the development of the electronic computer as occurring in waves. The first large wave was the mainframe era, when many people had to share single machines. (The mainframe era is covered in the section <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref216051" class="md-crosslink">The age of Big Iron</a>.) In this view, the minicomputer era can be seen as a mere eddy in the larger wave, a development that allowed a favoured few to have greater contact with the big machines. Overall, the age of mainframes could be characterized by the expression “Many persons, one computer.”</p><!--[P224]--><p>The second wave of computing history was brought on by the personal computer, which in turn was made possible by the invention of the microprocessor. (This era is described in the section <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/computer/History-of-computing#ref216069" class="md-crosslink">The personal computer revolution</a>.) The impact of personal computers has been far greater than that of mainframes and minicomputers: their processing power has overtaken that of the minicomputers, and networks of personal computers working together to solve problems can be the equal of the fastest supercomputers. The era of the personal computer can be described as the age of “One person, one computer.”</p><!--[P225]--><p>Since the introduction of the first personal computer, the <span id="ref724676"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/semiconductor" class="md-crosslink">semiconductor</a> business has grown to more than a quarter-trillion-dollar worldwide industry. However, this phenomenon is only partly ascribable to the general-purpose microprocessor, which accounts for about one-sixth of annual sales. The greatest growth in the semiconductor industry has occurred in the manufacture of special-purpose processors, controllers, and digital signal processors. These computer chips are increasingly being included, or embedded, in a vast array of consumer devices, including pagers, mobile telephones, music players, automobiles, televisions, digital cameras, kitchen appliances, video games, and toys. While the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Intel" class="md-crosslink">Intel Corporation</a> may be safely said to dominate the worldwide microprocessor business, it has been outpaced in this rapidly growing multibillion-dollar industry by companies such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Motorola-Inc" class="md-crosslink">Motorola, Inc.</a>; <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Hitachi-Ltd" class="md-crosslink">Hitachi, Ltd.</a>; <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Texas-Instruments-Incorporated" class="md-crosslink">Texas Instruments Incorporated</a>; <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/NEC-Corporation" class="md-crosslink">NEC Corporation</a>; and Lucent Technologies Inc. This ongoing third wave may be characterized as “One person, many computers.”</p><!--[P226]--></section><span id="ref216089" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216092" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724726"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/PDA" class="md-crosslink">Handheld digital devices</a></h2><p>The origins of handheld digital devices go back to the 1960s, when <span id="ref724678"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Alan-Kay" class="md-crosslink">Alan Kay</a>, a researcher at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Xerox-PARC" class="md-crosslink">Xerox’s Palo Alto Research Center</a> (PARC), promoted the vision of a small, powerful notebook-style computer that he called the <span id="ref724677"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Dynabook" class="md-crosslink">Dynabook</a>. Kay never actually built a Dynabook (the technology had yet to be invented), but his vision helped to catalyze the research that would eventually make his dream feasible.</p><!--[P227]--><p>It happened by small steps. The popularity of the personal computer and the ongoing miniaturization of the semiconductor circuitry and other devices first led to the development of somewhat smaller, portable—or, as they were sometimes called, luggable—computer systems. The first of these, the <span id="ref724679"></span>Osborne 1, designed by Lee Felsenstein, an electronics engineer active in the Homebrew Computer Club in San Francisco, was sold in 1981. Soon most PC manufacturers had portable models. At first these portables looked like sewing machines and weighed in excess of 20 pounds (9 kg). Gradually they became smaller (laptop-, notebook-, and then sub-notebook-size) and came with more powerful processors. These devices allowed people to use computers not only in the office or at home but also while traveling—on airplanes, in waiting rooms, or even at the beach.</p><!--[P228]--><p>As the size of computers continued to shrink and microprocessors became more and more powerful, researchers and entrepreneurs explored new possibilities in mobile computing. In the late 1980s and early ’90s, several companies came out with handheld computers, called <span id="ref724680"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/PDA" class="md-crosslink">personal digital assistants</a> (PDAs). PDAs typically replaced the cathode-ray-tube screen with a more compact <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/liquid-crystal-display" class="md-crosslink autoxref">liquid crystal display</a>, and they either had a miniature keyboard or replaced the keyboard with a stylus and handwriting-recognition software that allowed the user to write directly on the screen. Like the first personal computers, PDAs were built without a clear idea of what people would do with them. In fact, people did not do much at all with the early models. To some extent, the early PDAs, made by Go Corporation and Apple, were technologically premature; with their unreliable handwriting recognition, they offered little advantage over paper-and-pencil planning books.</p><!--[P229]--><p>The potential of this new kind of device was realized in 1996 when Palm Computing, Inc., released the <span id="ref724681"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Palm-Pilot" class="md-crosslink">Palm Pilot</a>, which was about the size of a deck of playing cards and sold for about $400—approximately the same price as the MITS Altair, the first personal computer sold as a kit in 1974. The Pilot did not try to replace the computer but made it possible to organize and carry information with an electronic <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/calendar" class="md-crosslink autoxref">calendar</a>, telephone number and address list, memo pad, and expense-tracking software and to synchronize that data with a PC. The device included an electronic cradle to connect to a PC and pass information back and forth. It also featured a data-entry system called “graffiti,” which involved writing with a stylus using a slightly altered alphabet that the device recognized. Its success encouraged numerous software companies to develop applications for it.</p><!--[P230]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="19228"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/15/23615-004-6599FB73.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/15/23615-004-6599FB73.jpg" alt="The Palm Pilot personal digital assistant (PDA)Introduced in March 1997, this PDA model was equipped with enough processing power to store and manipulate personal information, as well as handle the most common scheduling tasks." data-width="237" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The Palm Pilot personal digital assistant (PDA)Introduced in March 1997, this PDA model was equipped with enough processing power to store and manipulate personal information, as well as handle the most common scheduling tasks.</span><cite>Courtesy of 3Com Corporation</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML230]--><p>In 1998 this market heated up further with the entry of several established consumer electronics firms using <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Windows-OS" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Microsoft’s Windows</a> CE operating system (a stripped-down version of the Windows system) to sell <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/PDA" class="md-crosslink autoxref">handheld computer</a> devices and wireless telephones that could connect to PCs. These small devices also often possessed a communications component and benefited from the sudden popularization of the Internet and the World Wide Web. In particular, the BlackBerry PDA, introduced by the Canadian company Research in Motion in 2002, established itself as a favourite in the corporate world because of features that allowed employees to make secure connections with their company’s databases.</p><!--[P231]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="115615"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/54/122254-004-5CCA00C8.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/54/122254-004-5CCA00C8.jpg" alt="The BlackBerry personal digital assistant (PDA), manufactured by the Canadian company Research in Motion." data-width="264" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">The BlackBerry personal digital assistant (PDA), manufactured by the Canadian company Research in Motion.</span><cite>PRNewsFoto/Verizon Wireless/AP Images</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML231]--><p>In 2001 Apple introduced the iPod, a handheld device capable of storing 1,000 songs for playback. Apple quickly came to dominate a booming market for music players. The iPod could also store notes and appointments. In 2003 Apple opened an online music store, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/iTunes" class="md-crosslink">iTunes</a> Store, and in the following software releases added photographs and movies to the media the iPod could handle. The market for iPods and iPod-like devices was second only to cellular telephones among handheld electronic devices.</p><!--[P232]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="84965"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/81/91881-004-EC96ECAD.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/81/91881-004-EC96ECAD.jpg" alt="iPod" data-width="438" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">iPod</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Apple's fifth-generation iPod portable media player, 2005.</span><cite>Courtesy of Apple</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML232]--><p>While Apple and competitors grew the market for handheld devices with these media players, mobile telephones were increasingly becoming “smartphones,” acquiring more of the functions of computers, including the ability to send and receive <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/e-mail" class="md-crosslink">e-mail</a> and text messages and to access the Internet. In 2007 Apple once again shook up a market for handheld devices, this time redefining the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/smartphone" class="md-crosslink autoxref">smartphone</a> market with its iPhone. The touch-screen interface of the iPhone was in its way more advanced than the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/graphical-user-interface" class="md-crosslink">graphical user interface</a> used on personal computers, its storage rivaled that of computers from just a few years before, and its <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/operating-system" class="md-crosslink">operating system</a> was a modified version of the operating system on the Apple Macintosh. This, along with synchronizing and distribution technology, embodied a vision of ubiquitous computing in which personal documents and other media could be moved easily from one device to another. Handheld devices and computers found their link through the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Internet" class="md-crosslink">Internet</a>.</p><!--[P233]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="126817"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/17/128717-004-F9158469.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/17/128717-004-F9158469.jpg" alt="LG enV2 telephone by Verizon Wireless featuring a music player, games, mobile messaging, a camera, and a camcorder." data-width="550" data-height="365"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">LG enV2 telephone by Verizon Wireless featuring a music player, games, mobile messaging, a camera, and a camcorder.</span><cite>Verizon Wireless/PRNewsFoto/AP Images</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML233]--></section></section><span id="ref216089" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216093" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">One interconnected world</h2><span id="ref216089" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216094" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4">The <span id="ref724682"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Internet" class="md-crosslink">Internet</a></h2><p>The <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Internet" class="md-crosslink">Internet</a> grew out of funding by the U.S. Advanced Research Projects Agency (ARPA), later renamed the Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency (<a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Defense-Advanced-Research-Projects-Agency" class="md-crosslink">DARPA</a>), to develop a communication system among government and academic computer-research laboratories. The first network component, <span id="ref724683"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/ARPANET" class="md-crosslink">ARPANET</a>, became operational in October 1969. With only 15 nongovernment (university) sites included in ARPANET, the U.S. National Science Foundation decided to fund the construction and initial maintenance cost of a supplementary network, the <span id="ref724684"></span>Computer Science Network (CSNET). Built in 1980, CSNET was made available, on a subscription basis, to a wide array of academic, government, and industry research labs. As the 1980s wore on, further networks were added. In <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/North-America" class="md-crosslink autoxref">North America</a> there were (among others): BITNET (Because It’s Time Network) from IBM, UUCP (UNIX-to-UNIX Copy Protocol) from Bell Telephone, <span id="ref724695"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/USENET" class="md-crosslink">USENET</a> (initially a connection between <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Duke-University" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Duke University</a>, Durham, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/North-Carolina-state" class="md-crosslink autoxref">North Carolina</a>, and the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/University-of-North-Carolina" class="md-crosslink autoxref">University of North Carolina</a> and still the home system for the Internet’s many newsgroups), NSFNET (a high-speed <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/National-Science-Foundation" class="md-crosslink autoxref">National Science Foundation</a> network connecting supercomputers), and CDNet (in Canada). In Europe several small academic networks were linked to the growing North American network.</p><!--[P234]--><p>All these various networks were able to communicate with one another because of two shared protocols: the <span id="ref724715"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/TCP-IP" class="md-crosslink">Transmission-Control Protocol</a> (TCP), which split large files into numerous small files, or packets, assigned sequencing and address information to each packet, and reassembled the packets into the original file after arrival at their final destination; and the Internet Protocol (IP), a hierarchical addressing system that controlled the routing of packets (which might take widely divergent paths before being reassembled).</p><!--[P235]--><p>What it took to turn a network of computers into something more was the idea of the hyperlink: computer code inside a document that would cause related documents to be fetched and displayed. The concept of hyperlinking was anticipated from the early to the middle decades of the 20th century—in Belgium by <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Paul-Otlet" class="md-crosslink">Paul Otlet</a> and in the United States by Ted Nelson, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Vannevar-Bush" class="md-crosslink">Vannevar Bush</a>, and, to some extent, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Douglas-Engelbart" class="md-crosslink">Douglas Engelbart</a>. Their yearning for some kind of system to link knowledge together, though, did not materialize until 1990, when <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Tim-Berners-Lee" class="md-crosslink">Tim Berners-Lee</a> of England and others at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/CERN" class="md-crosslink">CERN</a> (European Organization for Nuclear Research) developed a protocol based on <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/hypertext" class="md-crosslink">hypertext</a> to make information distribution easier. In 1991 this culminated in the creation of the <span id="ref724719"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/World-Wide-Web" class="md-crosslink">World Wide Web</a> and its system of links among user-created pages. A team of programmers at the U.S. National Center for Supercomputing Applications, Urbana, Illinois, developed a <span id="ref724717"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Netscape-Navigator" class="md-crosslink">program</a> called a <span id="ref724718"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/browser" class="md-crosslink">browser</a> that made it easier to use the World Wide Web, and a spin-off company named <span id="ref724716"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Netscape-Communications-Corp" class="md-crosslink">Netscape Communications Corp.</a> was founded to commercialize that technology.</p><!--[P236]--><p>Netscape was an enormous success. The Web grew exponentially, doubling the number of users and the number of sites every few months. Uniform resource locators (URLs) became part of daily life, and the use of electronic mail (e-mail) became commonplace. Increasingly business took advantage of the Internet and adopted new forms of buying and selling in “cyberspace.” (Science fiction author William Gibson popularized this term in the early 1980s.) With Netscape so successful, Microsoft and other firms developed alternative Web browsers.</p><!--[P237]--><p>Originally created as a closed network for researchers, the Internet was suddenly a new public medium for information. It became the home of virtual shopping malls, bookstores, stockbrokers, newspapers, and entertainment. Schools were “getting connected” to the Internet, and children were learning to do research in novel ways. The combination of the Internet, e-mail, and small and affordable computing and communication devices began to change many aspects of society.</p><!--[P238]--><p>It soon became apparent that new <span id="ref724720"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/software" class="md-crosslink">software</a> was necessary to take advantage of the opportunities created by the Internet. <span id="ref724721"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Sun-Microsystems-Inc" class="md-crosslink">Sun Microsystems</a>, maker of powerful desktop computers known as workstations, invented a new <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/object-oriented-programming" class="md-crosslink autoxref">object-oriented programming</a> language called <span id="ref724722"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Java-computer-programming-language" class="md-crosslink">Java</a>. Meeting the design needs of embedded and networked devices, this new language was aimed at making it possible to build applications that could be stored on one system but run on another after passing over a network. Alternatively, various parts of applications could be stored in different locations and moved to run in a single device. Java was one of the more effective ways to develop software for “smart cards,” <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/plastic" class="md-crosslink autoxref">plastic</a> debit cards with embedded computer chips that could store and transfer electronic funds in place of cash.</p><!--[P239]--></section><span id="ref216089" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref279800" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref1071153"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/e-commerce" class="md-crosslink">E-commerce</a></h2><p>Early enthusiasm over the potential profits from <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/e-commerce" class="md-crosslink">e-commerce</a> led to massive cash investments and a “dot-com” boom-and-bust cycle in the 1990s. By the end of the decade, half of these businesses had failed, though certain successful categories of online business had been demonstrated, and most conventional businesses had established an online presence. Search and online advertising proved to be the most successful new business areas.</p><!--[P240]--><p>Some online businesses created <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/niches" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="niches">niches</a> that did not exist before. <span id="ref1071154"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/eBay" class="md-crosslink">eBay</a>, founded in 1995 as an online auction and shopping <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Web-site" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Web site</a>, gave members the ability to set up their own stores online. Although sometimes criticized for not creating any new wealth or products, eBay made it possible for members to run small businesses from their homes without a large initial investment. In 2003 Linden Research, Inc., launched <span id="ref1071155"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Second-Life" class="md-crosslink">Second Life</a>, an Internet-based <span id="ref1071157"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/virtual-reality" class="md-crosslink">virtual reality</a> world in which participants (called “residents”) have cartoon-like avatars that move through a graphical environment. Residents socialize, participate in group activities, and create and trade virtual products and virtual or real services. Second Life has its own currency, the Linden Dollar, which can be converted to U.S. dollars at several Internet currency exchange markets. Second Life challenged the boundary between real and virtual economies, with some people earning significant incomes by providing services such as designing and selling virtual <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/dress-clothing" class="md-crosslink autoxref">clothing</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/furniture" class="md-crosslink autoxref">furniture</a>. In addition, many real world businesses, educational institutions, and political organizations found it advantageous to set up virtual shops in Second Life.</p><!--[P241]--><p>Maintaining an Internet presence became common for conventional businesses during the 1990s and 2000s as they sought to reach out to a public that was increasingly active in online social <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/communities" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="communities">communities</a>. In addition to seeking some way of responding to the growing numbers of their customers who were sharing their experiences with company products and services online, companies discovered that many potential customers searched online for the best deals and the locations of nearby businesses. With an Internet-enabled smartphone, a customer might, for example, check for nearby restaurants using its built-in access to the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/GPS" class="md-crosslink">Global Positioning System</a> (GPS), check a map on the Web for directions to the restaurant, and then call for a reservation, all while en route.</p><!--[P242]--><p>The growth of online business was accompanied, though, by a rise in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/cybercrime" class="md-crosslink">cybercrime</a>, particularly <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/identity-theft" class="md-crosslink autoxref">identity theft</a>, in which a criminal might gain access to someone’s <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/credit-card" class="md-crosslink autoxref">credit card</a> or other identification and use it to make purchases.</p><!--[P243]--></section><span id="ref216089" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref279801" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref1071158"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/social-network" class="md-crosslink">Social networking</a></h2><p>Social networking services emerged as a significant online phenomenon in the 2000s. These services used software to <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/facilitate" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="facilitate">facilitate</a> online communities, where members with shared interests swapped files, photographs, videos, and music, sent messages and chatted, set up <span id="ref1071159"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/blog" class="md-crosslink">blogs</a> (Web diaries) and discussion groups, and shared opinions. Early social networking services included Classmates.com, which connected former schoolmates, and Yahoo! 360°, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Myspacecom" class="md-crosslink">Myspace</a>, and SixDegrees, which built networks of connections via friends of friends. By 2018 the leading social networking services included <span id="ref1071186"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Facebook" class="md-crosslink">Facebook</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Twitter" class="md-crosslink">Twitter</a>, Instagram, <span id="ref1071188"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/LinkedIn" class="md-crosslink">LinkedIn</a>, and Snapchat. LinkedIn became an effective tool for business staff recruiting. Businesses began exploring how to exploit these networks, drawing on social networking research and theory which suggested that finding key “influential” members of existing networks of individuals could give access to and credibility with the whole network.</p><!--[P244]--><p>Blogs became a category unto themselves, and some blogs had thousands of participants. Trust became a commodity, as sharing opinions or ratings proved to be a key to effective blog discussions, as well as an important component of many e-commerce Web sites. <span id="ref1071191"></span>Daily Kos, one of the largest of the political blogs, made good use of ratings, with high-rated members gaining more power to rate other members’ comments; under such systems, the idea is that the best entries will survive and the worst will quickly disappear. The vendor rating system in eBay similarly allowed for a kind of self-policing that was intended to weed out unethical or otherwise undesirable vendors.</p><!--[P245]--></section><span id="ref216089" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref216095" data-level="4"><h2 class="h4"><span id="ref724725"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/ubiquitous-computing" class="md-crosslink">Ubiquitous computing</a></h2><p>The combination of the connectedness of the Internet with the ability of new <span id="ref1071192"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/microprocessor" class="md-crosslink">microprocessors</a> that can handle multiple tasks in parallel has inspired new ways of programming. Programmers are developing software to divide computational tasks into subtasks that a program can assign to separate processors in order to achieve greater efficiency and speed. This trend is one of various ways that computers are being connected to share information and to solve complex problems. In such <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/distributed-computing" class="md-crosslink autoxref">distributed computing</a> applications as airline reservation systems and automated teller machines, data pass through networks connected all over the world. Distributed computing promises to make better use of computers connected to ever larger and more complex networks. In effect, a distributed network of personal computers becomes a <span id="ref1071193"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/supercomputer" class="md-crosslink">supercomputer</a>. Many applications, such as research into protein folding, have been done on distributed networks, and some of these applications have involved calculations that would be too demanding for any single computer in existence.</p><!--[P246]--><p>Considerable work in research laboratories is extending the actual development of embedded microprocessors to a more sweeping vision in which these chips will be found everywhere and will meet human needs wherever people go. For instance, the <span id="ref724724"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/GPS" class="md-crosslink">Global Positioning System</a> (GPS)—a <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/satellite-communication" class="md-crosslink autoxref">satellite communication</a> and positioning system developed for the U.S. military—is now accessible by anyone, anywhere in the world, via a special commercial GPS receiver. In conjunction with various computer-mapping softwares, GPS can be used to locate one’s position and plan a travel route, whether by car or on foot.</p><!--[P247]--><p>Some researchers call this trend ubiquitous computing or <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/pervasive" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="pervasive">pervasive</a> computing. Ubiquitous computing would extend the increasingly networked world and the powerful capabilities of distributed computing—i.e., the sharing of computations among microprocessors connected over a network. (The use of multiple microprocessors within one machine is discussed in the article <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/supercomputer" class="md-crosslink">supercomputer</a>.) With more powerful computers, all connected all the time, thinking machines would be involved in every facet of human life, albeit invisibly.</p><!--[P248]--><p><span id="ref1071194"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Xerox-PARC" class="md-crosslink">Xerox PARC</a>’s vision and research in the 1960s and ’70s eventually achieved commercial success in the form of the mouse-driven graphical user interface, networked computers, laser printers, and notebook-style machines. Today the vision of ubiquitous computing foresees a day when microprocessors will be found wherever humans go. The technology will be invisible and natural and will respond to normal patterns of behaviour. Computers will disappear, or rather become a transparent part of the physical environment, thus truly bringing about an era of “One person, many computers.”</p><!--[P249]--></section></section></section><span class="md-signature"><a href="/contributor/Paul-A-Freiberger/4495">Paul A. Freiberger</a></span><span class="md-signature"><a href="/contributor/Michael-R-Swaine/4493">Michael R.
